Master Thesis 
Environmental assessment of surplus food management 
in the retail sector: The benefits of donation and 
recovery as animal feed 
 
 
 
Paola Federica Albizzati 
s151212 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Supervisors 
Davide Tonini 
Thomas F. Astrup 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
1st of June 2017 
==PAGE:2==
 
 
ii 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Technical University of Denmark 
Department of Environmental Engineering 
2800 Kongens Lyngby, Denmark 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:3==
 
iii 
 
Abstract 
Every year, 1.3 billion tonnes of food are wasted globally. Food waste represents an economic, social, and 
environmental problem. Economically, it implies wasted investments and higher expenses for consumers. 
Socially, food waste reduction should be one of the priorities to tackle hunger and improve food safety both 
for people in need and for poorer countries. Environmentally, it implies the use of a great amount of limited 
resources and greenhouse gas emissions. For these reasons, together with an increasing demand for food and 
a growing population, food waste and losses should be addressed. The European Commission has decided that 
by 2030 the food waste per capita at household and at retail sector should be halved. The retail sector, which 
is the focus of the study, is an important stage along the food supply chain. In fact, retailers centralise a great 
amount of food, and are the connection between producers and consumers. Hence, it is very important to 
address food waste at this stage of the chain. The study focuses on surplus food generated at the retail sector 
in France, i.e. unsold food by the retailers. The EU Regulations in respect to food donations have been 
implemented in France to ease the donations via transfer slips, and to incentivize them through tax credits. 
However, this is not the main trend in the Member States. In Denmark, for example, it is still difficult to donate 
food as the EU Regulations have been applied with stricter rules, and there are no fiscal incentives. Donation 
of food is on the second highest level of the waste hierarchy, which is proposed and supported in the mentioned 
EU Regulations. The most favoured option proposed by the waste hierarchy is prevention, followed by 
redistribution (i.e. food donations), recycle (i.e. animal feed), recovery (i.e. incineration and anaerobic 
digestion), and disposal (i.e. landfilling). The goal of the study is to assess the environmental performance of 
the different management strategies proposed by the hierarchy. However, the disposal option was not included 
in this study for two main reasons: the first is that EU directives have been implemented in the individual states 
to ban landfilling of (fresh untreated) organic waste; and the second, because there already is an enormous 
amount of literature showing the environmental impacts of landfilling of food waste, and it was decided, 
therefore, to focus the research on other pathways for which limited literature exists. The results obtained from 
the life cycle assessment support the waste hierarchy: prevention, indeed, leads to the largest savings. 
Moreover, food production is identified as the main contributor to the impacts. On this basis, it is crucial to 
promote and implement food waste prevention measures. However, whenever it is not possible to avoid the 
generation of surplus food, and whenever food is still safe and suitable for human consumption, redistribution 
measures have to be applied to prevent the usage of limited resources in vain. Further, if food is still safe but 
it is not suitable for human consumption, the surplus food should be used as animal feed. Finally, if the food 
is spoilt and it is not suitable neither for humans nor for animals, recovery should be implemented: the food 
waste can be used to produce heat and electricity that displace corresponding heat and electricity produced 
from fossil sources.

1. Introduction 
Every year one-third of the food produced for human consumption is wasted (FAO, 2011). Food loss and food 
waste represent an economic, social, and environmental issue (Richter et al., 2015). In fact, economically, food 
waste implies wasted investments, lower incomes for farmers and higher expenses for consumers (Lipinski et 
al., 2013). Socially, food loss and food waste must be reduced to tackle hunger and improve food security both 
for poorer countries and people with lower incomes (Richter et al., 2015). Environmentally, food waste and 
food loss imply the use of a great amount of resources (i.e. land, water, energy, etc.) causing unnecessary 
greenhouse gas emissions (FAO, 2011). For these reasons, together with a growing population and an 
increasing demand for food, food waste and food loss must be addressed (Richter et al., 2015). Therefore, the 
European Commission has taken action and has decided that by 2030 the food waste per capita at household 
and at retail sector has to be cut by 50% (European Commission, 2017). 
Food loss and food waste refer to food produced for human consumption but not finally consumed by people 
(Lipinski et al., 2013). However, food waste and food loss refer to different concepts. According to Lipinski 
et al. (2013), food loss is food of lower quality (e.g. spoilt) or food lost before it reaches the consumer due to 
the mishandling of the product, lack of infrastructure and storage, or limitation in marketing. Moreover, food 
loss occurs before the final product is either manufactured or reaches the retail sector (FAO, 2017). On the 
other hand, food waste refers to good quality food suitable for human consumption that is discarded before or 
after it spoils (Lipinski et al., 2013) and occurs at the retail and household stage (FAO, 2017). Moreover, food 
waste is further defined as avoidable (e.g. leftovers), partly avoidable (e.g. skin of a potato) and not avoidable 
(e.g. bones) (Frohnmaier et al., 2013). Avoidable food waste is further defined as вҖңfood and drink items which 
at some point, prior to being thrown out, was edibleвҖқ, in accordance with Quested et al. (2009). However, 
while food loss highly depends on technical issues, food waste is dependent on behavioural aspects. Another 
important definition in the context of the wholesale and retail sector (and thus for this study) is food surplus, 
which is defined as food and drinks that have not been sold or are not marketable for several reasons, but are 
suitable for human consumption (Council of the European Union, 2017). 
It is important to reduce food waste and food loss and this can be done in several ways. According to Lipinski 
et al. (2013), the following solutions could be applied: 
1. Food redistribution or donation: food given away voluntary to, e.g., food banks that then redistribute 
it to people in need; 
2. Evaporative coolers: prolongation of shell life of food; 
3. Plastic storage bags: use of plastic bags, especially вҖңPurdue Improved Cowpea StorageвҖқ (PICS) 
composed by three bags and reusable; 
4. Small metal silos: use of hermetic silos for long-term storage; 
5. Plastic crates: reusable, avoid the spoilage of the good; 
6. Food date labelling: clarification on label as вҖңuse-byвҖқ, вҖңsell-byвҖқ and вҖңbest beforeвҖқ; 
7. Consumer awareness campaigns: education and clarification on food date labelling; 
8. Reduced portion sizes: reduced portions in restaurants, etc.  
According to Lipinski et al. (2013) вҖңFood Redistribution or DonationвҖқ is considered as a possible solution to 
reduce food waste and food loss. Indeed, food redistribution is recommended as a sustainable disposal 
technology for food waste by the EU (Salemdeeb et al., 2016). The guidelines provided by the European Waste 
Framework Directives follow the waste hierarchy (European Commission, 2008), which is shown in Figure 1.  
==PAGE:10==
 
 
2 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
The most favoured option is Reduction, which concerns all the measures applied on a material or product 
before it becomes waste (WRAP, 2011). The focus is to reduce the amount of waste via reuse and the extension 
of the life-span of products. Moreover, Reduction includes avoidance (e.g. buy less products), reduction (e.g. 
store products for a longer time), and reuse (e.g. sell and buy used items) of the products (WRAP, 2011). The 
second most favoured option for food waste and food loss management is Redistribution to charities or to 
consumers with lower incomes, for example (Salemdeeb et al., 2016). Recycle refers to waste materials or 
products that are processed into a product, material or substance that will be used for the previous purpose or 
a new one (e.g. animal feed) (WRAP, 2011). Recovery implies the recovery of the energy stored in the products 
through combustion and energy recovery (i.e. incineration), anaerobic digestion, gasification, or pyrolysis 
(WRAP, 2011). Disposal is the least favoured option as it contemplates the use of landfills, which imply the 
production of a great amount of greenhouse gases and, therefore, has been phased out according to the 
European CommissionвҖҷs directive of 2014 (European Commission, 2016). 
1.1 State of the art 
According to FAO (2011) 1.3 billion tonnes of food are wasted globally every year. Developed countries waste 
much more food than developing ones: in Europe and North-America 95-115 kg/year of food is wasted per 
capita by consumers, whilst in sub-Saharan Africa and South/Southeast Asia only 6-11 kg/year are wasted. 
According to this study, food losses occur along the entire Food Supply Chain (FSC). However, in developed 
countries food waste and food loss are generated at the end of the FSC, more specifically at the consumer level, 
while in developing countries food is lost mainly at the early and middle stages of the FSC. In fact, in 
developing countries food losses are due to technical issues (e.g. unsuitable storage, lack of infrastructures, 
etc.), whereas in the developed world food is either lost or wasted due to cultural or behavioural issues (e.g. 
confusion on labelling, quality standards, etc.). In the study by FAO (2011), several causes and prevention 
measures related to food waste and food loss are reported and are herein summarized in Table 1. 
 
 
Figure 1: The food waste hierarchy. Figure adapted from Salemdeeb et al. (2016). 
==PAGE:11==

According to Parfitt et al. (2010) great losses occur at the end of the FSC due to behavioural issues. The food 
products that are mostly wasted are fresh fruit and vegetable, followed by bakery products, dairy products, 
and, finally, meat and fish. The main factors contributing to a high production of food waste at household level 
were identified. It is stated that the composition of the family influences the production of food waste per 
capita: bigger families waste less than smaller ones; families with children waste more than the ones without, 
although it depends on the age of the children; single-person householders tend to waste more food. The 
income of the household also plays a role: households with higher salaries tend to waste more food. 
Furthermore, Parfitt et al. (2010) state that in developed countries more food will be wasted by 2050 due to 
high aesthetic standards, low prices of food prices and an increasing lack of knowledge regarding how food is 
produced. Therefore, it is necessary to educate consumers to reduce food waste in developed countries. A 
cultural shift of the consumers may lead to a reduction in food waste. However, to achieve food waste reduction 
it is necessary to implement legislations that would lead to a more sustainable food production.  
According to Lipinski et al. (2013), it is necessary to implement regulations and legislations that would lead 
to a reduction in food waste and food loss. Therefore, five recommendations are proposed. The first one is 
focused on the development of a standardized method for countries and companies to measure and monitor 
consistently the food that is either wasted or lost. This method or protocol should be global so that every 
country and all the companies would be able to implement it. The data collected would be transparent and 
comparable across countries/companies. It is important to know how much food is wasted and lost, otherwise 
==PAGE:12==
 
 
4 
 
little can be done to implement reduction policies. The second recommendation proposes to set targets on food 
waste and food loss reduction. The targets could either be global, national, sub-national (i.e. provinces and 
cities), or for companies. These targets would have to be time-bounded and could lead to an increased 
awareness on food waste and food loss. The third recommendation is focused on investing more in developing 
countries on postharvest losses (i.e. losses at the handling and storage stages) rather than crop production. In 
fact, currently, only 5% of the investments on agricultural research are focused on postharvest losses and most 
of the losses in developing countries occur at this stage of the FSC. On the other hand, in developed countries, 
most of the losses occur at the final stages of the FSC: more than 60% of the food waste and loss occurs at the 
retail stages, households and restaurants (Lipinski et al., 2013). This problem is addressed with the fourth 
recommendation, where it is suggested to create entities that would work in partnership with retailers, 
businesses, and individuals to reduce food waste and food loss. The last recommendation suggests 
collaborative initiatives that include many actors to achieve food loss and food waste reduction. 
In the study by Richter et al. (2015) it is stressed how important it is to address the problem of food losses due 
to the increasing population and, consequently, demand for food. In fact, it is stated that the prevention of food 
loss could be beneficial both from an economical point of view, from a social (e.g. tacking hunger) and an 
environmental (e.g. lower use of raw materials) one. In the study, several are the causes spotted leading to food 
loss generation. In the manufacturing companies, spoilt products, overproduction, inefficient management, 
exceedance of the best-before-date, but also exogenous factors (e.g. weather) are identified as the main causes 
of food losses. Richter et al. (2015) suggest several measures to reduce the food losses generated at 
manufacturing companies: (i) food donations, (ii) waste recycling (e.g. use of food surplus as animal feed in 
zoos), (iii) market predictions, (iv) better management of cold chain, (v) improve packaging, (vi) collaboration 
between retail and suppliers, (vii) education of the personnel. However, it is stated in the study that the greatest 
loss of food along the FSC occurs at the end of it, i.e. at households. In fact, to obtain a significant reduction 
of food losses it is highly important to educate consumers, as it was also stated in the study by FAO (2011). 
According to Papargyropoulou et al. (2014) the reduction of food waste and food loss can only be achieved by 
implementing a sustainable production and consumption of food and by dealing with the generation of food 
waste and food loss throughout the FSC. To achieve this purpose, it is suggested in the study to apply the food 
waste hierarchy. According to this method, the most favourable option is prevention followed by redistribution 
of food losses to people with low incomes and reuse as animal feed. Food waste and food loss imply the use 
of unnecessary resources, such as water, land, etc. Moreover, agriculture is responsible of ca. 22% of the global 
GHG emissions, of which 18% are due to livestock production. Land use changes due to demand for land, 
where agriculture plays a major role, are responsible for additional 10-15% of the global GHG emissions. 
Therefore, the prevention of food waste and food loss could lead to significant environmental savings and 
avoid the use of unnecessary resources, such as water, land, etc. The prevention of food waste could not only 
lead to environmental benefits, but also economic and social ones.  
In the study by Oldfield et al. (2016) both the utilisation and the prevention of wasted food and food residues 
(WFFR) are assessed using the Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) methodology. The scenarios considered in the 
study are: (i) landfilling, (ii) composting, (iii) waste prevention, (iv) anaerobic digestion, and (v) incineration. 
The impact categories considered in the assessment are: Global Warming, Acidification Potential, and 
Eutrophication Potential. The lowest impacts for all three impact categories were obtained for the waste 
prevention scenario, whereas the second lowest for all three impact categories were obtained for the anaerobic 
digestion of WFFR. According to the sensitivity analysis, the most significant process for the anaerobic 
digestion, composting, and incineration was the avoidance of the production of grid electricity, whilst for the 
waste prevention was food production. Moreover, it was found in the study that food production is highly 
dependent on the type of food produced. The impacts for all the considered impact categories were higher 
when the quantity of meat and fish, dairy and mixed food but also vegetables, which accounts for 
approximately 25% of the wasted food, were increased/decreased by 10%. However, if the production of these 
==PAGE:13==
 
5 
 
foods is avoided, higher environmental savings are not assured. In fact, a great amount of land would be 
available and could be harvested with less or more polluting crops. Moreover, waste prevention would lead 
also to a great monetary saving and this money could be spent on goods more polluting than the avoided foods. 
These are unintended consequences of prevention strategies and are generally called вҖңindirect (or sometimes 
also induced or rebound) effectsвҖқ (Martinez-Sanchez et al. 2016). 
In the study by Lebersorger et al. (2014) the focus is on food losses generated in 612 retail outlets in Austria 
over one year. The food categories investigated in the study are: fruit and vegetables, dairy products, and bread 
and pastry. Food is lost due to apparent flaws of the products, exceedance of the best before or sell-by date, 
damaged packaging or breakage (i.e. damaged packaging and food product). Fruit and vegetables are mainly 
lost for apparent flaws, dairy products for breakages, and bread and pastry for exceedance of the best before 
or sell-by date.  In the study, the data are expressed as monetary values rather than masses, as the latter were 
more uncertain. Therefore, food losses are expressed as food loss rates, which are calculated as the ratio in 
Euro cost price of the food loss and its sale. The total food loss in monetary values for fruit and vegetables, 
dairy products, and bread and bakery amounts to 81% of the total monetary value of food loss; fruit and 
vegetables account for 53%, dairy products for 22% and bread and pastry for 25%. However, the amount of 
food lost per category (i.e. fruit and vegetables, dairy products, bread and pastry) exhibits a seasonal variation: 
during winter, more bread and pastry are wasted, whilst during summer more fruits and vegetables, and dairy 
products are wasted. Of the 612 retails, 62% donate surplus food, but only 7% of it is donated to social services; 
therefore, only a small percentage of the food loss does not become food waste. Of the total monetary value of 
the food donated, 53% accounts for dairy products, bread and pastry account for 30%, while fruit and 
vegetables only account for 17%. Donation could reduce waste quantities generated by retails and, 
consequently, decrease the costs for waste management.  
Eriksson et al. (2015) state that the results obtained by many life cycle assessment studies follow the waste 
hierarchy. However, most of the existing studies do not include the highest levels of the hierarchy, i.e. donation 
and prevention. Therefore, the study investigates the global warming potential of six different scenarios: 
landfilling, incineration, anaerobic digestion, composting, animal feed production, and donation of food to 
charities. To perform the LCA the functional unit is defined as follows вҖңremoval of 1 kg of food waste 
(including packaging) from the supermarketsвҖқ. Due to the variety of properties that characterize food waste, 
it is difficult to define it as a homogeneous entity. Hence, only five different products describe the food waste 
and they are chosen either due to their contribution in terms of greenhouse gas emissions (stewing beef) or 
mass (bananas, bread, grilled chicken, and iceberg lettuce). The geographical scope of the study is Uppsala, a 
town in Sweden. It is important to notice that currently in Sweden feeding animals with by-products (e.g. food 
waste) is highly restricted and the supermarkets that want to implement this strategy have to be registered as a 
feed producer and have to separate all the food of animal origin from the ones of a vegetative one. However, 
in the study, grilled chicken and beef are included in the animal feed as it is considered only a theoretical 
exercise. Animals are usually fed with oats and soybeans that provide energy and proteins, respectively. In the 
study, when system expansion is performed, it is assumed that vegetable foods (i.e. bananas, bread, and iceberg 
lettuce) replace oats based on their energy content, while animal products (i.e. stewing beef and grilled chicken) 
replace soybeans based on their protein content. Regarding the system expansion of the donation of food to 
charities, in the study it is assumed that the donated food displaces food that would have otherwise been bought 
by charities and people in need. It is assumed that all the donated food displaces bread based on its energy 
content. This hypothesis was made as bread is one of the cheapest food products that are available in Sweden 
and it does not require any preparation unlike other food products with a high-energy content (e.g. pasta, rice, 
etc.). However, this hypothesis is very uncertain; hence, it was tested with the sensitivity analysis and it was 
found that the results obtained were highly dependent on the assumption made. The results obtained from the 
study, however, showed that whenever a method prioritised in the waste hierarchy was chosen, the greenhouse 
gas emissions were lower than the ones produced by less prioritised ones. Hence, based on the obtained results 
on the global warming potential, it was suggested that supermarkets should reduce the loss of animal products, 
==PAGE:14==
 
 
6 
 
donate vegetables and bread, and send to anaerobic digestion the wet food items and to incineration the dry 
ones.  
The above-mentioned literature shows that most of the research in this field focused on finding the causes that 
lead to the production of food waste and food loss. Most of the recommendations suggested focus on 
improvements throughout the FSC and on behavioural changes of consumers. To reduce food waste and food 
loss, many studies recommend implementing the food waste hierarchy. To choose the best waste management 
system from an environmental perspective the European Waste Framework Directive suggests using the LCA 
as methodology (Buratti et al., 2014). Yet, as highlighted by Eriksson et al. (2015), few are the studies that 
investigate higher levels of the waste hierarchy. Thus, more research is needed on this field to evaluate the 
actual environmental benefits obtained by waste prevention compared to other waste management systems 
(Eriksson et al., 2015). In particular, we see three important aspects that need special attention from the 
research. The first relates to the LCA approach used for addressing the benefits of food donation; the study by 
Eriksson et al. (2015) shows that more research is required regarding the scenario of donation of food losses 
generated by supermarkets. In fact, the assumption that was made in the study regarding the substituted 
(avoided, in LCA terms) food was very rough and very sensitive. The second relates to the datasets used for 
food waste products (items). Food waste is very heterogeneous and it is complex to characterize it. Thus, it is 
necessary to expand the knowledge on the characteristics of the food products regarding both the nutritional 
properties (e.g. energy, vitamins, etc.) and the ones related to the energy content (e.g. methane potential, low 
heating value, etc.). Finally, the third aspect relates to identifying technical and legislative barriers, at country 
level, limiting the implementation of optimal food waste (or, more in general, surplus food) management 
options (e.g. hierarchy) and, on this basis, to propose possible solutions for improvements and optimization of 
the management.  
1.2 Overview of the study 
The current study focuses on the management of the food surplus generated by French retails (supermarkets). 
As mentioned earlier, herein food surplus is defined as food and drink that have not been sold or are not 
marketable for several reasons, but are suitable for human consumption (Council of the European Union, 
2017). The data about the surplus food was provided by PH. (the name cannot be disclosed due to 
confidentiality agreements between DTU and the company), a French company working with the retail sector 
in valorisation of food surplus. Original data were expressed as monetary values (вӮ¬). The monetary values 
were converted into masses (kg) based on the consumption patterns of French people. This was done to avoid 
a top-down approach when evaluating the environmental impacts of the considered scenarios; in fact, input-
output LCAs are based on aggregating activities in a limited number of sectors (Nielsen et al., 2001), leading 
to a possible overestimation of the results (Yang et al., 2017). Further, a detailed database of food products 
and related chemical/biochemical/physical properties was elaborated to consider a realistic food mix in the 
assessment.  
The main trend in France is either to redistribute the surplus food or use it as animal feed. Therefore, it was 
decided to consider and compare the following scenarios: incineration, anaerobic digestion, use as animal feed, 
redistribution of surplus food, and prevention. Further, a perturbation analysis was performed together with a 
sensitivity analyses. This mainly focused on testing the assumption used in the baseline for the French marginal 
electricity mix. Moreover, minimum-maximum ranges in relation to the waste composition (in terms of 
specific food products composing the mix, e.g. shares of pork, chicken, beef, cheese, aubergines, bread, etc.) 
were investigated for all the scenarios for each impact category. 
The current EU Regulations related to food donations and their key barriers are discussed together with 
solutions implemented in different Member States. Further, the implementation of the EU Regulations in 
France and Denmark are presented to show the differences in the two countries.  
==PAGE:15==
 
7 
 
1.3 Goals definition 
The specific objectives of the study are listed herein: 
вҖў 
Perform, using LCA, an environmental impact assessment of the surplus food management 
system of French supermarkets; 
вҖў 
Assess, using LCA, the environmental impacts of several strategies according to the food 
waste hierarchy, focusing on the highest levels of it; 
вҖў 
Analysis of the factors contributing to the LCA environmental impacts; 
вҖў 
Contextualisation of the obtained LCA results in respect to similar studies on prevention; 
вҖў 
Elaboration of a consequential LCA-oriented approach to quantify benefits from 
redistribution; this translates into identifying the marginal mixture of foods for the 
Redistribution scenario; 
вҖў 
Elaboration of a database that includes all the relevant food products and their 
chemical/physical/biochemical properties; 
вҖў 
Consider a changed mix of foods when assessing the environmental impacts of the scenarios; 
вҖў 
Contextualisation of the results in respect to the current EU Regulations regarding food 
donations, use as animal feed and anaerobic digestion; 
вҖў 
Based on the French case and the analysis of the current Danish food waste regulations, 
suggest recommendations on how to improve the surplus food management system in the 
Danish retail sector. 
The results of this study are intended to support current/envisioned EU policies related to the implementation 
of the waste hierarchy. It should be noted that decisions involving the surplus food management at retails could 
influence many systems that interact between each other, e.g. land use, agriculture, distribution, waste sector. 
On this basis, the decision context to which this study aims to is the so-called вҖңsituation BвҖқ, i.e. a meso/macro-
level decision support context, according to the categories defined by European Commission (2010). 
 
 
==PAGE:16==
 
 
8 
 
2.Scope Definition 
2.1 Function and Functional Unit 
This study uses consequential LCA to assess and compare the environmental impacts of different management 
strategies for food surplus in the French retail sector. Consequential (or change-oriented) LCA (and associated 
datasets) is used in this study as the consequential approach is the most suitable to support decision-making 
processes. On this basis, marginal (instead of average) data for technologies and products are used, i.e. those 
actually affected by a change in demand (non-constrained; Weidema et al., 2003; Weidema et al. 1999). For 
further explanations of consequential vs. attributional LCA, the reader is referred to existing literature on the 
subject (Weidema et al. 2003; Weidema et al. 1999; Finnveden et al. 2009). 
The functional unit is defined as follows: вҖңManagement of 1 tonne of surplus food (including packaging) 
generated at the retail sector in FranceвҖқ. The management options considered in the study are based on the 
food waste hierarchy and are the following: 
вҖў 
Incineration 
вҖў 
Anaerobic Digestion 
вҖў 
Use as Animal Feed 
вҖў 
Redistribution 
вҖў 
Prevention 
The geographical scope is France as French supermarkets are considered in the study. Moreover, current 
technologies are considered. The temporal scope of the study is the short-term period 2015-2020. 
2.2 Presentation of the scenarios 
The scenarios considered in the LCA are herein presented to have a better understanding of section 2.3 where 
the system boundaries are explained. For more details regarding the marginal technologies/products refer to 
section 2.4. The complete outlines of the considered scenarios can be found in Appendix A. An overview of 
the scenarios considered in the current study is provided in Figure 2.

10 
 
 
2.2.1 Incineration 
According to the definitions provided earlier, food surplus becomes food waste once it enters the waste 
management system. In this scenario, the food becomes waste once it is collected by the collection trucks that 
transport the waste to a sorting facility. The waste that is herein considered includes both food and packaging, 
as stated in the functional unit. Therefore, the packaging can either be incinerated together with the food or 
recycled. The types of packaging considered in the study are either made of plastic or paper. Hence, the plastic 
is sorted from the paper in a sorting facility and, then, recycled. Instead, the waste sent to incineration is used 
to produce electricity and heat, while the bottom ashes are used in road constructions. 
2.2.2 Anaerobic Digestion 
According to the definitions provided earlier, food surplus becomes food waste once it enters the waste 
management system. The surplus food generated by the supermarket is addressed as food waste as it is 
transported to a sorting facility by a collection truck. After sorting the waste, three streams are identified: (i) 
only food waste that will be digested, (ii) only packaging that will be recycled, and (iii) food residues and 
packaging, which will be incinerated.  
According to Tonini et al. (2013), only 80% of the food is sent to anaerobic digestion, whilst the residuals are 
assumed to be incinerated. This sorting efficiency is used in this study. The digestion of food waste produces 
two outputs: the biogas and the digestate. The biogas is used to produce electricity and heat displacing the 
respective marginal technologies, while the digestate is used as fertilizer instead of the conventional ones (refer 
to section 2.4 for further explanations regarding the marginal electricity, heat, and mineral fertilizers). 
Regarding the two other streams (i.e. food and packaging, and only packaging), the same system is used as in 
the Incineration scenario. 
2.2.3 Animal Feed 
Since 2002, it is banned to feed with non-vegetative food, animals intended for human consumption (European 
Commission, 2002). In fact, using food containing animal products as animal feed, induces the transmission 
of diseases among the animals for human consumption. However, according to the regulation of 2002 
(European Commission, 2002), if it can be demonstrated that there is no contamination with animal products, 
the surplus food can be used as animal feed. In this study, only fruit and vegetables, and bread are intended for 
animal consumption. Hence, the food that is not suitable for animal consumption is transported with a 
collection truck to a sorting facility. Here, the food waste is further sorted. In fact, three waste management 
systems are analysed: (i) anaerobic digestion of only food waste, (ii) incineration of packaging and food, and 
(iii) recycling of the packaging. The description of the three mentioned systems is the same as the one presented 
for the Anaerobic Digestion and the Incineration scenarios. 
The use of food surplus as animal feed, avoids the production of conventional animal feeds (i.e. maize grain 
and soybean), which implies use of land, fertilizers and water (WRAP, 2015). For further explanations 
regarding the marginal animal feed and land use changes refer to section 2.4 and 2.5, respectively.  
2.2.4 Redistribution 
The food surplus generated at the supermarket is transported to charities/food banks, i.e. institutions that 
arrange the redistribution of the food for people in need. According to Alexander et al. (2008), only 60% of 
the surplus food redistributed is actually used in charities or redistributed to people in need (i.e. actually 
reaching the target-people and substituting other food). This efficiency factor is used in this study to account 
for eventual system losses. The redistributed food displaces the food that would have been bought otherwise 
by charities or people in need. Therefore, the production of the food products and the land use changes 
==PAGE:19==
 
11 
 
connected to it are avoided. More details regarding the marginal mix of food and the land use changes can be 
found in section 2.4 and 2.5, respectively.  
The food residues (i.e. 40% due to system inefficiency) and the packaging enter the waste management system 
and become waste. A collection truck transports the waste to a sorting facility where the waste can either 
undergo anaerobic digestion (only food), incineration (food and packaging) or recycling (only packaging). The 
description of the three waste management systems is the same as the one presented in the previous sections. 
2.2.5 Prevention 
In this scenario, the environmental savings due to the avoidance of the generation of the surplus food at the 
retail sector are considered.  In the scenario, the food production, the land use changes associated with the food 
production, the transport of the food products and associated refrigeration (when it applies), and storage and 
cooling at the supermarket are included. In section 2.5, detailed explanations about the land use changes are 
provided. 
2.3 System Boundaries 
The system boundaries include: agricultural production of food products, land use changes, storage and 
cooling, packaging, transportation and refrigeration, collection, surplus food management and waste 
management. Therefore a вҖңcradle-to-graveвҖқ approach is applied as the whole life cycle of the food products is 
considered. However, the boundaries of the LCA of each scenario depend on the management option selected. 
It is important to note that the Prevention scenario includes the agricultural production of food products and 
their transport, the production and transport of packaging, cooling and storage, and the land use changes due 
to the production of food, i.e. all activities occurring prior to the generation of the food surplus, while the other 
scenarios do not. Accounting for these activities in the Prevention scenario (and not in the remaining scenarios) 
is correct in order to consistently compare the environmental impacts of the assessed scenarios, following 
previous similar studies and guidelines (see, among the others, Martinez-Sanchez et al. 2016, Gentil et al. 
2010). 
The multifunctionalities of the systems are handled through system expansion. As mentioned earlier, the 
chosen LCI modelling framework is based on the consequential principle: marginal products and technologies 
should therefore be identified. This is done in section 2.4.  
2.3.1 Prevention  
The avoided production of the food surplus generated at the retail sector is herein considered. The LCA 
accounts for the agricultural production of the food products, the land use changes due to the production of the 
food, the transport of the food and its refrigeration, the production and transport of packaging, and the storage 
and cooling at the supermarket. Further explanations regarding the land use changes are provided in section 
2.5. It is important to notice that the processes considered in the prevention scenario are not modelled in the 
other scenarios due to system equivalence.  
The system boundaries are displayed in Figure 3 with a black, dashed line, while the processes included in the 
scenario are represented with grey boxes with a dashed line as all of them are avoided. 
 
Figure 3: System boundaries (black dashed line) of the Prevention scenario. The processes included in the scenario are displayed with 
a grey dashed line as all of them are avoided. Note that вҖңexpвҖқ stands for вҖңexpansionвҖқ, whilst вҖңintвҖқ stands for вҖңintensificationвҖқ.

the supermarket, prior to the generation of the food surplus, are not considered due to system equivalence. The 
tilted black lines indicate that the mentioned processes are cut by the system but would otherwise be included 
in the system. The displacement of the marginal electricity, heat, packaging and road construction materials

2.4 Identification of the marginal technologies/products 
With respect to the electricity, the marginal electricity was assumed to be equal to the mix for the electricity 
production in France, which is mainly characterised by renewable energies (IEA, 2017). The LCI of the process 
was based on Ecoinvent 3.3 (Wernet et al., 2016). 
With respect to the heat, the marginal heat was assumed to be produced by natural gas with a boiler fan burner. 
The LCI of the process was based on Ecoinvent 2 (Frischknecht et al., 2005). 
With respect to the packaging, it was assumed that the marginal paper packaging was paper (Cardboard and 
mixed paper) and the LCI was based on Merrild et al. (2008), whilst the marginal plastic packaging was either 
PET or PP (depending on products; see Appendix G) and their LCI was based on Ecoinvent 3.3 (Wernet et al., 
2016) data for the production of these types of packaging. The packaging for liquid food items was assumed 
to be PE and the LCI was based on Ecoinvent 3.3 (Wernet et al., 2016). 
With respect to the road construction material, it was assumed that the marginal material was gravel and natural 
aggregates. The LCI was based on Birgisdottir et al. (2005).  
With respect to the mineral fertilizers, it was assumed that the marginal fertilizers were urea, diammonium 
phosphate and potassium chloride based on Tonini et al. (2016). Based on the N, P and K content of the 
digestate, the substitution ratios were calculated. The LCI of the production of the marginal fertilizers was 
based on Ecoinvent 3.3 data (Wernet et al., 2016).  
With respect to the animal feed, the marginal one was assumed to be maize grain, rich in energy, and soybean, 
rich in proteins. According to Dalgaard et al. (2008), soymeal is produced together with soy oil. Thus, if less 
soymeal is required, less soy oil will be produced. Soymeal is displaced by the food surplus, whilst the soy oil 
is replaced by palm oil. Therefore, when balancing the digestible energy content (i.e. SFU) and the protein 
==PAGE:24==
 
 
16 
 
content of the feeds, it is necessary to take into account the soymeal-palm fruit loop following the approach 
provided in Dalgaard et al. (2008). For the detailed calculations see Appendix B. 
With respect to the marginal food mix for the redistribution scenario, this marginal mixture is defined as what 
would otherwise be purchased by consumers (i.e. charities, people, or third parties). To define the marginal 
mixture it was decided, for the sake of simplicity, to divide the foods redistributed by retailers into five macro-
categories according to the macro-categories and food products described in section 4: 
1. Fruit and Vegetables: all the food items of the macro-category Fruit and Vegetables; 
2. Grain: all the food items included in the macro-categories Dry savoury, Bakery, and the food items 
frozen bread and frozen savoury; 
3. Meat: all the food items included in the macro-categories Charcuterie, Meat, Poultry, Fish and 
molluscs, and the food items eggs, frozen meat, frozen poultry and frozen fish; 
4. Dairy: all the items of the macro-category Fresh dairy products, except for eggs; 
5. Other: all the food items of the macro-category Dry sweet, Pastry, Liquids, and the food items 
frozen fruit and vegetable, frozen pastry and gourmet. 
It is important to note that the criteria used to assign the food products to the macro-categories Fruit and 
Vegetables, Grain, Meat and Dairy are based on the document by the World Health Organization (2012), while 
the remaining food products of the redistributed food were assigned to the category Other. For Other, it was 
assumed that no substitution takes place, as these products consist of a mix of food products that are not 
considered to be a necessity within a basic human diet. 
It was assumed that the marginal mix would be composed by the cheapest food products of Fruit and 
Vegetables, Grain, Meat and Dairy existing in the market. Based on EUROSTAT (2015) the cheapest food 
products were selected. The data, together with the obtained results, are summarized in Table 2. It is important 
to note that for the macro-category Fruit and Vegetables four items were considered, as fruits and vegetables 
are aggregated in one category. It was decided to select the two cheapest food products to represent a varied 
mix, but, for the sake of simplicity, it was decided to limit the quantity of the products to two per macro-
category as people have many choices at the supermarket and it would be difficult to forecast what they would 
buy.

The substitution of the marginal mix is based on a weight basis. On this basis, the share (expressed as [%]) of 
Fruit and Vegetables, Grain, Meat, and Dairy of the redistributed mixture of foods, was calculated. It is 
important to notice that, for this calculation, the food products included in Fruit and Vegetables, Grain, Meat, 
and Dairy, were selected accordingly to the definition presented above based on the WHO (2012). The 
calculated substitution factor for Fruit and Vegetables was 0.3, for Grain 0.26, for Meat 0.12, and for Dairy 
0.21.  
2.5 Land Use Changes 
The change in management of a land, e.g. from forest to arable land, is referred as a land use change. Due to 
deforestation, land use changes account for 12% of the global greenhouse gases emissions (Herzog, 2009). 
Thus, it is important to account for this impact when performing a LCA. In this study only indirect LUC 
impacts are accounted for, this being the most important in relation to LCA results as showed in previous 
studies (Tonini et al. 2012; Schmidt et al. 2013). While direct LUC (dLUC) may be important when assessing 
local regional land-use strategies and when assessing impacts of individual cropping systems, the order of 
magnitude of the impacts is typically 1 (or more) order of magnitude lower than iLUC. For simplicity, from 
now onwards we will refer to iLUC as to LUC. 
To calculate the LUC impacts due to the demand for arable land (for the production of food), the approach of 
Tonini et al. (2016) was applied. The model assumes that the effects related to the demand of land are global, 
as the market for agricultural products is global. The model calculates (indirect) land use changes due to 
demand for land, i.e. it calculates the upstream impacts of demanding (thus using) land regardless of the use 
as such. On this basis, all food products and all countries demanding for land are contributing to the global 
pressure on forests/biomes (and on the consequent land use changes), regardless of the type of food products 
(crops) and location. This means that demanding 1 m2 for wheat in Europe or 1 m2 for soybean in South 
America are equally important with respect to indirect land use changes (i.e. the upstream consequence of 
demanding land is the same) and are punished with the same iLUC factor per m2. In the model by Tonini et al. 
(2016), an additional demand for land is compensated by a response from expansion and from intensification. 
Expansion accounts for 25% of the total response, whilst intensification accounts for 75%. The combined 
effect of intensification and expansion provides the impact due to land use changes. These are accounted in 
the categories Climate changes, Terrestrial Acidification, Eutrophication Marine. In the other categories the 
iLUC impacts are not included due to data limitations. 
The complete list of the land use changes associated to the production of the food items can be found in 
Appendix C. 
 
 
==PAGE:26==
 
 
18 
 
2.6 Impact assessment method and normalization 
The environmental assessment was performed using the software EASETECH (Clavreul et al. 2014). Midpoint 
impact categories are considered in the current study: Climate change (Forster et al., 2007), Human Toxicity, 
Cancer effects (Rosenbaum et al., 2011), Terrestrial Acidification (SeppГӨlГӨ et al., 2006), Eutrophication 
Marine (Struijs et al., 2009), Ecotoxicity freshwater (Rosenbaum et al., 2011), Depletion of abiotic resources, 
fossil (van Oers et al., 2002). The mentioned impact categories were addressed as they are those mainly 
addressed in bio-based products LCAs (Broeren et al., 2017).  
To compare the results obtained across the impact categories considered and to express them on a common 
scale, normalization is performed using the normalization factors presented in Table 3.

3.Food Database  
One of the objectives of this study is to create a database comprising detailed information regarding the 
properties of food products. The database is based on the Frida Fooddata elaborated by the National Food 
Institute and the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The food products are characterised according to 
the following nutrients: macronutrients, factors (e.g. protein conversion factor), vitamins, minerals and trace 
elements, organic acids, carbohydrate fractions, fatty acids, amino acids, sterols, and biogenic amines (DTU 
FГёdevareinstituttet, 2016). The database includes the range of food products in Denmark and, to preserve its 
quality, it is continuously updated by the Danish Veterinary and Food Administration, food companies and 
food retailers (DTU FГёdevareinstituttet, 2016).  
As stated by Eriksson et al. (2015), waste is characterized by various properties and, therefore, it is very 
heterogeneous. Thus, to be able to include all the food products characterizing the surplus food, it is necessary 
to consider all the properties that are relevant to calculate the environmental impacts. To perform the LCA, it 
was necessary to calculate the following properties: Lower Heating Value on the dry basis (LHVdb), methane 
potential, biogenic carbon and biogenic carbon anaerobically digestible, digestible energy, and the 
Scandinavian Feed Unit (SFU).  The calculations performed to define these properties are presented in the 
following sections, while the complete calculations for the rearrangement of all the considered components in 
the LCA and the hypotheses made can be found in Appendix D. 
For modelling purposes, an average composition of the considered food products was calculated and 
considered. In fact, per each food product, more than one product was available in the Frida Fooddata (e.g. 
for cheese, there are more than 50 products) Thus, after calculating all the necessary properties for all the food 
products in the database, according to the equations presented in Appendix D, average food products were 
calculated (e.g. average cheese). For further details on the structure of the database and of the вҖңaverageвҖқ 
database, please refer to Digital Appendix 1 and Digital Appendix 2, respectively.

2010). The LHV is expressed as [MJ/kgTS] and, according to Fowler et al. (2009), the LHV is calculated as 
follows:

4.Elaboration of the data 
PH. provided the information regarding the food surplus generated at the French retail sector. The data 
available referred to the surplus food of 20 supermarkets from September 2016 to November 2016. The macro-
categories considered in the study are the following: Dry sweet, Dry savoury, Frozen food, Charcuterie 
traiteur, Fresh dairy products, Fruit and Vegetables, Poultry, Meat, Cheese, Gourmet, Pastry, Bakery, Fish, 
and Liquids. 
The data were expressed as monetary values (i.e. вӮ¬), and represented the wholesale price, i.e. the price at which 
supermarkets buy the goods (Quora, 2016). It is assumed that the wholesale price of a product not only covers 
the costs to produce the product itself, but also the costs to produce its packaging. Therefore, the functional 
unit of this study was referred to 1 tonne of food surplus including packaging.  
As it was intended to use a bottom-up approach to evaluate the environmental impacts of different management 
strategies, it was necessary to convert the provided amounts from Euros to kilograms. Firstly, based on 
FranceAgriMer (2016), Camera di Commercio Roma (2015), and information provided by PH., the wholesale 
prices were collected and expressed as [вӮ¬/kg]. Secondly, to have a detailed classification of the food surplus, 
the food consumption pattern in France was characterised based on Agence FranГ§aise de SГ©curitГ© Sanitaire des 
Aliments (2009), Interfel (2017), Maison du Lait (2017), Les Fabricants de Biscuits & GГўteaux de France 
(2015) and FranceAgriMer (2015). Finally, the wholesale prices were weighted accordingly to the French food 
consumption pattern. The conversion factors used are presented in Table 4. For the complete explanation of 
the calculations and hypotheses made, see Appendix E. 
As it was already mentioned, PH. provided data for 20 French supermarkets from September 2016 to 
November 2016. The LCA was performed for the surplus food of an average supermarket over three months, 
which was calculated based on the provided data and applying the assumed conversion factors. The results 
obtained together with the corresponding monetary value, and the conversion factors used, are presented in 
Table 4. Note that herein food surplus accounts both for the food and its packaging, accordingly to the 
functional unit. The complete dataset can be found in Appendix F.  Notice that in Table 4, 5 the numbers are 
rounded off to the first digit.

The surplus food, in terms of mass, is mainly composed by Fruit and Vegetables (29.6%), Fresh dairy products 
(20.7%) and Bakery (20.3%), whilst, in monetary values, the highest losses due to the generation of surplus 
food are found for Fruit and Vegetables (24.7%), Charcuterie traiteur (16.2%), Fresh dairy products (11.7%), 
and Gourmet (10.0%). According to Teuber et al. (2016), the majority of surplus food in terms of mass is found 
for bread and bakery products, and fruit and vegetables. This is also supported by the study of Parfitt et al. 
(2010): the majority of the surplus food accounts for fruit and vegetables, followed by bakery, dairy, meat, and 
fish. This trend was reflected in our results. However, it was found that the surplus food for Fresh dairy 
products was larger than the one of Bakery; this might be due to the assumed wholesale price of Fresh dairy 
products (i.e. 1.019 вӮ¬/kg), which was low due to the high consumption of milk and its low price. Further, 
according to Teuber et al. (2016), the majority of the surplus food in monetary values is found for cold cuts, 
fresh meat, fresh ready meals, and fresh dairy products. However, the mentioned trend is not reflected in the 
results obtained: this might be due to a different definition of the macro-categories and the food products 
included in each one of them. 
The surplus food is herein expressed as food and its packaging. Thus, the amount of packaging was calculated 
based on Tonini et al. (2017) for each food product of the considered macro-categories. It is important to notice 
that in France fruit and vegetables are sold without any packaging. Therefore, its packaging was set to 0 kg. 
The types of packaging considered in the assessment are: paper, PET, PP, and PE. The surplus food accounts 
for 97.7% food and 2.3% packaging (0.4% paper, 0.6% PET, 0.5% PP, and 0.8% PE).  The amount of 
packaging, food and of surplus food (i.e. food and packaging), together with the share of the food on the surplus 
food are presented in Table 5. All the assumptions made for the type of packaging chosen for each food product 
==PAGE:31==

be found in Appendix H.

5.Life Cycle Inventory Analysis 
Herein, the quality of the data and the assumptions made regarding the LCI of the processes used are discussed. 
5.1 Data quality 
The data provided by PH. on the surplus food, on which the LCA is based, are primary data as the 
measurements are performed on-site and, for this reason, the data can be considered of very high quality 
(Laurent et al., 2016).  
The data related to the efficiency of the technologies (e.g. efficiency of the electricity recovery of the 
incinerator) and to the cooling and storage at the supermarket, are based on the literature. As the data used was 
specific to the considered processes, they can be considered as medium quality data, according to the definition 
provided by Laurent et al. (2016).  
The values related to the transport of food to the charity and to the sorting facilities, were assumed: thus they 
can be considered of very low quality, according to the definition provided by Laurent et al. (2016). 
The quality of the data and its source are summarized in Table 6. 
Table 6: List of data quality and data source for the considered processes.

5.2 Life Cycle Inventory  
The Life Cycle Inventory (i.e. LCI) of the processes considered in the LCA was based on Ecoinvent 3.3 
(Wernet et al., 2016). It is important to note that the processes considered in the model were not always 
available in the database. Therefore, whenever it was possible, the LCI was based on the literature, and 
processes from Ecoinvent 3.3 were used to create the missing process in EASETECH. If no information was 
available, then an assumption was made using existing processes in Ecoinvent 3.3. The decision tree used 
when collecting the LCIs of the considered processes is presented in Figure 8. 
==PAGE:33==
 
25 
 
 
Figure 8: Decision tree used when collecting the LCIs of the considered processes. 
The processes for which their LCI was based on the literature are: Production of chestnuts, based on Rosa et 
al. (2016); Production of pasta, based on Lo Giudice et al. (2014); Production of orange juice, based on Sense 
(2012); Production of watermelon, based on Shamshirband et al. (2015). The LCI found in the literature did 
not always account for nitrogen emissions due to the use of N-fertilizers and manure, which, therefore, had to 
be estimated. The calculations were based on the method presented by Tonini et al. (2016). Of N-emissions, 
due to the application of N-fertilizers, 1.5% are as N2O-N, 2% as NH3-N, 1.1% as NOx-N, and 20% as NO3-
N, whilst the one coming from the application of manure are 1.5% as N2O-N, 11% as NH3-N, 1.1% as NOx-
N, and 51% as NO3-N (Tonini et al., 2016). Notice that also indirect emissions as N2O-N are considered and 
calculated, both for the application of N-fertilizers and of manure (Tonini et al., 2016). 
The processes for which the LCI was based on an assumption are listed in Appendix I. The main assumptions 
made are herein presented: 
- 
LCI missing of the food products belonging to the macro-category Fruit and Vegetables: it was 
decided to model the missing LCIs as another fruit or vegetable belonging to the same family. If this 
criterion could not be applied, then a best guess was made;  
- 
LCI missing of the food products belonging to the macro-category Poultry: it was decided to model 
all the food products included in the macro-category as chicken, as no other process was available in 
Ecoinvent 3.3; 
- 
LCI missing of the food products belonging to the macro-category Meat: it was decided to model the 
majority of the missing processes as pork as it is the most consumed food product in the considered 
macro-category. 
- 
LCI missing of the food products belonging to the macro-category Fish: it was decided to model the 
processes as chicken as it is the meat product with the lowest environmental impacts. 
Further research on this topic should be also focused on finding appropriate LCIs for the missing processes, 
especially for the macro-category Fish. In fact, unrealistic impacts are attributed to the production of the food 
products belonging to this macro-category. Thus, the impacts related to the production of the mentioned food 
products might be overestimated in the obtained results. 
As mentioned above, the database used is Ecoinvent 3.3. Only consequential processes were considered, as 
the current LCA is change-oriented. Moreover, Rest of the World (RoW) or global (GLO) 
processes/technologies were included. The complete list of the processes used in the model, can be found in 
Appendix J. 
 
 
==PAGE:34==
 
 
26 
 
 
5.3 Assumptions 
In respect to the substitution of mineral fertilizers, based on Boldrin et al. (2009) and other studies (e.g. Tonini 
et al., 2013), it was assumed that organic digestate substitutes 40% conventional N-mineral fertilizers based 
on the nitrogen content whilst for phosphorus and potassium the substitution is 100% (Hansen et al., 2006). 
The storage and cooling at the retail sector was based on LCA Food Database (2007). According to The 
Guardian (2016), all the French supermarkets with an area of 400 m2 or more, have to donate food. As no 
information was provided regarding the area of the considered supermarkets, it was assumed that they belong 
to the category вҖңsmall retail shopвҖқ, and the associated datasets from LCA Food Database (2007) was used 
accordingly. Based on this, the electricity and heat consumption were calculated. The consumption factors 
were available for milk, frozen pomes frites, potatoes and pasta only. Hence, it was assumed that Dry sweets, 
Dry savoury, Gourmet required the same heat and electricity as pasta; Charcuterie traiteur, Fresh dairy 
products, Poultry, Meat, Cheese, Fish, and Liquids as milk; Frozen food as frozen pomes frites, and Fruit and 
Vegetables, Pastry, and Bakery as potatoes, as they all have a short shelf-life. 
The transport of the food products from the production stage to the retail sector was based on the information 
provided by Ecoinvent 3.3 (Wernet et al., 2016). In respect to Fruit and Vegetables, the transport includes the 
food losses (i.e. 12%) from the retail sector to the consumer (Gustavsson et al., 2013). Hence, only 88% of the 
transport reported by Ecoinvent 3.3 was considered in this study for the mentioned macro-category in order to 
avoid double counting. In fact, after a detailed discussion with Ecoinvent developers and after a careful review 
of the reports on transport, it became clear that this 12% loss accounted by Ecoinvent includes leakages 
occurring from the gate of processing (post-industry) to the consumer households, thus including in this chain 
the distribution sector itself, e.g. retails. This 12% loss considered in Ecoinvent data should therefore be 
cancelled to avoid double counting.  
In respect to the production of meat, the impacts due to the slaughterhouse processing have not been included 
as only animal farming was accounted for in the assessment. Hence, the results obtained for the production of 
Meat, and Poultry are underestimated. 
 
==PAGE:35==
 
27 
 
6.Results  
The results herein presented are: 
вҖў 
Environmental impacts of the considered management strategies (i.e. Incineration, Anaerobic 
digestion, Animal feed, Redistribution, and Prevention), together with the analytical standard 
deviation; 
вҖў 
Perturbation analysis based on the вҖңOATвҖқ method; 
вҖў 
Sensitivity analysis of the baseline results when varying the marginal electricity; 
вҖў 
Minimum-maximum ranges for each impact category for each scenario following extreme variations 
of the surplus food composition. 
The contributors to the impacts are: Land Use Change (including both expansion and intensification), 
Packaging (including production of the packaging), Transport, Refrigeration, Cooling and Storage (including 
transport and refrigeration of the products, cooling and storage at the retail sector), Food production (including 
food production), Feed production (including the use as animal feed), Waste Management and Collection 
(including incineration, anaerobic digestion, recycling of the packaging, and collection of the waste). Total is 
the net sum of both the savings and the burdens: all the values above the zero line are considered environmental 
burdens, whilst the ones below savings. 
6.1 Characterised results at midpoint level 
The obtained results, together with the analytical standard deviation, are shown in Figure 9. Notice that in 
Figure 9, Depletion of abiotic resources, fossil is addressed as Resource depletion. Except for Ecotoxicity, 
freshwater, the results follow the waste hierarchy: the largest savings come from waste prevention, followed 
by redistribution, animal feed, and waste to energy (both incineration and anaerobic digestion). Furthermore, 
the avoided production of food is the main contributor to the savings for all the impact categories considered 
in the assessment. 
For the environmental category Climate change, the savings ranged between -87.4 (for Anaerobic digestion), 
and -2306.8 (for Prevention) kgCO2-eq per tonne of surplus food. Prevention leads to the largest savings due 
to the avoidance of all the activities associated to the life cycle of the food. Moreover, of the total impact, 
approximately 73% of the savings are due to the avoided production of the food items, and 22% to the avoided 
land use changes. The scenario focused on the anaerobic digestion of the food waste has lower savings than 
the one focused on incineration. This is due to the lower impact of the French electricity mix (high share of 
renewables) that gives a low GW substitution factor. Also, the hypotheses made on the efficiency of both 
electricity and heat recovery from anaerobic digestion and on the characteristics of the substrate (i.e. food 
waste) in terms of water content may play a role favouring combustion over biological treatment, energy-wise. 
Moreover, it should also be hold in mind that food waste may undergo pre-treatments to increase the yield of 
the biogas production (Kondusamy et al., 2014), which is not accounted for in this assessment. 
For the environmental category Human toxicity, carcinogenic, the savings ranged between -1.57E-6 (for 
Incineration) and 5.05E-5 (for Prevention) CTUh per tonne of surplus food. The largest saving comes from 
Prevention: 95% of the savings are due to the avoided production of the food, which requires the use of 
pesticides and herbicides. Anaerobic digestion has larger savings than Incineration due to lower emission of 
heavy metals. 
For the environmental category Terrestrial acidification, the results ranged between 1.83 (for Anaerobic 
digestion) and -25.4 (for Prevention) molH+-eq per tonne of surplus food. Prevention leads to the largest 
savings, mainly due to avoided production of food (ca. 87%) and avoided land use changes (ca. 10%). This is 
due to the avoided use and application of mineral fertilizers (intensification part of the response to the demand 
for land) that lead to the emissions of NOx and SOx (Tonini et al., 2017). The scenario focused on anaerobic 
==PAGE:36==
 
 
28 
 
digestion leads to a burden, mainly because of the low savings from (French marginal) energy substitution and 
because of the emissions following on-land application of digestate. 
For the environmental category Eutrophication marine, driven by nitrogen leaching, the results ranged 
between 2.2 (for Anaerobic digestion) and -15.9 (for Prevention) kgN-eq per tonne of surplus food. The savings 
linked to Prevention are mainly due to the avoided production of food (ca. 75%) and avoided land use changes 
(24%). Anaerobic digestion induces a burden on the environment due to the leaching of the nitrogen related to 
the use of digestate on land. (Tonini et al., 2017). Incineration, Animal feed, and Redistribution also induce a 
burden on the environment due to Waste Management and Collection. The anaerobic digestion of food is also 
included in the scenarios focused on animal feed production and redistribution. In the scenario focused on the 
incineration of the food items, the impact is due to the emissions of nitrogen compounds to the environment 
(mainly NOx) that exceeds those of the substituted (French marginal) energy processes.. 
For the environmental category Ecotoxicity, freshwater, the savings ranged from -81.4 (for Incineration) to -
3259 (for Redistribution) CTUe per tonne of surplus food. The savings linked to Redistribution are mainly due 
to the avoided production of food (ca. 94%). Only for this impact category, Redistribution performs better than 
Prevention. This is due to the difference in the composition between the surplus food prevented in the 
prevention scenario and the marginal mix of food displaced in the redistribution scenario (because of the 
donation). In fact, in the Redistribution, the high savings are related to the avoided production of Grains, in 
particular of rice (see composition of marginal mix in Table 2). Rice production has an impact on Ecotoxicity, 
freshwater of 16.2 CTUe/kg due to the use of propanil, an herbicide. Rice, instead, accounts only for 0.7% in 
the mix of food considered in the Prevention scenario. Thus, the benefits from avoiding the production of rice 
are not as visible as in the Redistribution scenario. 
For the environmental category Depletion of abiotic resources, fossil, the savings ranged from -2450.8 (for 
Anaerobic digestion) to -18220 (for Prevention) MJ per tonne of surplus food. Prevention leads to the highest 
savings, which are mainly due to avoided production of food (79%) that requires a high amount of fossil 
resources. Moreover, both in the Redistribution (ca. 6%) and in the Prevention (ca. 8%) scenario, the avoided 
production of packaging is relevant. In fact, the production of plastic packaging would also be prevented, 
saving oil. Anaerobic digestion has lower savings compared to Incineration: this might be due to the hypothesis 
made on the efficiency of both electricity and heat recovery. 
To calculate the standard deviation, the method presented by Bisinella et al. (2016) was applied. The variance 
was calculated analytically assuming a triangular distribution of the parameters. Further details on the 
methodology are given in Appendix K. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:37==
 
29 
 
   
Figure 9: Results obtained for the impact categories considered. The standard deviation is displayed as well. The results are all 
calculated for 1 tonne of surplus food generated at the retail sector in France. Notice that Depletion of abiotic resources, fossil
is herein addressed as Resource depletion.

6.2 Perturbation analysis 
The perturbation analysis was performed following the вҖңone-at-a-timeвҖқ (OAT) approach (Bisinella et al., 
2016). According to this method, each parameter is changed one at a time keeping the other ones fixed to 
evaluate which parameters are the most sensitive in the scenario. For these parameters, it is necessary to 
improve the quality of the data. The parameters considered in the model were increased by 10%. It is important 
to note that, the parameters related to the food and packaging quantities were not modified to fulfil the 
functional unit (i.e. 1 tonne of surplus food and packaging). The complete list of the parameters and the method 
applied, can be found in Appendix K. 
Varying the parameters, it is possible to evaluate the sensitivity ratio (SR) and the sensitivity coefficient (SC). 
The former express the ratio of two relative changes, whilst the latter the ratio of two absolute changes 
(Bisinella et al., 2016). Based on Bisinella et al. (2016), the SR and the SC are defined as follows:

6.2.1 Incineration 
Herein, the results obtained for the Incineration are presented and show in Figure 10, where Depletion of 
abiotic resources, fossil is addressed as Resource depletion. The parameters involved in the scenario are: the 
distance of the collection (Transport), whether packaging is recycled or incinerated (Management_packaging), 
the efficiency of the electricity recovery (Electricity_IN), and the efficiency of the heat recovery (Heat_IN). 
The most sensitive parameter is the one related to the efficiency of the heat recovery, followed by the one 
related to the efficiency of the electricity recovery. Especially for the impact category Terrestrial acidification 
the mentioned parameters are very sensitive: the SR of Heat_IN is 5.3, while the one of Electricity_IN is 1.3.

6.2.2 Anaerobic digestion 
The results obtained for the Anaerobic digestion are herein presented and shown in Figure 11, where Depletion 
of abiotic resources, fossil is addressed as Resource depletion. The parameters involved in the scenario are: 
the distance of the collection (Transport), the biogas yield (Biogas_yield), the efficiency of the heat recovery 
from the anaerobic digestion (Heat_BIO), the efficiency of the electricity recovery from the anaerobic 
digestion (Electrcity_BIO), the efficiency of the heat recovery from the incineration (Heat_IN), the efficiency 
of the electricity recovery from the incineration (Electrcity_IN), the share of food going to anaerobic digestion 
(Sorting_BIO), and whether packaging is recycled or incinerated (Management_packaging). For the 
environmental impact Climate change, the most sensitive parameter is Heat_BIO (SR=0.5), which is connected 
to the displacement of the marginal heat production. For the environmental categories Human toxicity, 
carinogenic (SR=0.9), Terrestrial acidification (SR=1), Eutrophication marine (SR=1), and Ecotoxicity, 
freshwater (SR=0.9), the most sensitive parameter is the share of food going to anaerobic digestion that 
determines the amount of digestate produced and used on land, which is relevant for the mentioned impact 
categories. For the environmental category Resource depletion (SR=0.8), the most sensitive parameter is the 
yield of the biogas production, which is related to the displacement of the marginal heat and electricity of fossil 
origins.

6.2.3 Animal Feed 
The results obtained for Animal Feed are herein presented and shown in Figure 12, where Depletion of abiotic 
resources, fossil is addressed as Resource depletion. The parameters involved in the scenario are: production 
of soybean (Soybean), production of maize grain (Maizegrain), production of palm fruit (Palmfruit), distance 
of the collection (Transport), yield of the anaerobic digestion (Biogas_yield), the efficiency of the electricity 
and heat recovery from incineration (Electricity_IN and Heat_IN, respectively), the efficiency of the electricity 
and heat recovery from incineration (Electricity_BIO and Heat_BIO, respectively), share of food going to 
anaerobic digestion (Sorting_BIO), whether packaging is recycled or incinerated (Management_packaging), 
whether food is digested or incinerated (Management_Waste), the share of land use changes related to 
intensification (LUC_int), the land use changes related to the cultivation of soybean, maize grain and palm 
fruit (Soybean_Land, Maizegrain_Land, Palmfruit_Land, respectively). For the environmental category 
Climate change, the most sensitive parameters are LUC_int (SR= -1), Maizegrain (SR=0.6), and 
Maizegrain_Land (SR= -0.4). Notice that negative SRs are associated with lower savings. In fact, for example, 
if the area required to cultivate the maize grain increases, deforestation increases inducing effects on climate 
change. For the environmental impact Human toxicity, carcinogenic, the most sensitive parameters are 
Management_waste (SR= -0.53), Sorting_BIO (SR=0.58), and Maizegrain (SR=0.26). An increase of 10% of 
Management_waste would lead to lower savings as more food would be incinerated and, as mentioned in 
section 6.1, incineration performs worse than anaerobic digestion when considering the mentioned impact 
category. For the environmental category Terrestrial acidification, the most sensitive parameters are 
Maizegrain (SR=0.95), LUC_int (SR=0.5), and Maizegrain_Land (SR= -0.5). The need for more land for

6.2.4 Redistribution 
The results obtained for the Redistribution are herein presented and shown in Figure 13, where Depletion of 
abiotic resources, fossil is addressed as Resource depletion. The parameters involved in the scenario are: the 
substitution factor for the selected fruit and vegetables/grains/meat/dairy and the correspondent packaging

Yogurt_Land, Rice_Land, Apple_Land, Banana_Land, Carrot_Land, Potato_Land, Pasta_Land), the 
electricity and heat required for cooling and storage at the retail sector (Refrigeration_El and 
Refrigeration_Heat, respectively), the distance for the collection and to the charity (Transport and 
Transport_RED, respectively), the share of food undergoing anaerobic digestion (Sorting_BIO), the efficiency 
of the electricity and heat recovery from incineration (Electricity_IN and Heat_IN, respectively), the efficiency 
of the electricity and heat recovery from anaerobic digestion (Electricity_BIO and Heat_BIO, respectively), 
whether food waste is digested or incinerated (Management_waste), whether packaging is recycled or 
incinerated (Management_packaging), the share of land use changes related to intensification (LUC_int). For 
the environmental category Climate change, the most sensitive parameters are LUC_int (SR= -0.5) and 
Grain_Prices (SR=0.43). For the categories Human toxicity, carcinogenic, Terrestrial acidification,

6.3 Marginal electricity 
The marginal electricity assumed in the baseline scenario represents the mix of electricity production in France. 
Currently, the majority of the electricity is produced by renewable sources, such as wind energy, and nuclear 
(Treyer, 2015; IEA, 2017). The marginal heat, instead, was assumed to be produced from natural gas, a fossil 
source. As shown from the results presented in Appendix M, the savings due to the displacement of the 
marginal electricity are low, and are lower than the ones obtained when displacing the marginal heat. On this 
basis, it is not beneficial to produce energy from food waste.  
It was decided to test how the results would change if the marginal electricity was of fossil origin. Hence, 
electricity produced from natural gas in France was chosen as marginal electricity. It is important to note that 
this change was applied only to Incineration, Anaerobic digestion, Animal feed, and Redistribution, as, in these 
scenarios, electricity is displaced by the energy produced from food waste. The results obtained are 
summarized in Table 7, where both the results obtained for the baseline and for the tested scenario are 
presented. Notice that Prevention is included in the results shown both in Table 7 and Figure 15 for 
completeness and that Depletion of abiotic resources, fossil is addressed as Resource depletion.
