For the environmental category Climate change, the results ranged between -479.2 (for Anaerobic digestion) 
and -2306.8 (for Prevention) kgCO2-eq per tonne of surplus food. When considering fossil marginal electricity, 
the savings obtained from anaerobic digestion and incineration of food waste increased by 448% and 166%, 
respectively. Indeed displacing marginal electricity that includes energy produced by renewable energies has 
a low impact on Climate change (Eriksson et al., 2017), while substituting electricity from fossil production 
has vice versa a greater impact. 
For the environmental category Human toxicity, carcinogenic, the results ranged between -1.4E-06 (for 
Incineration) and -5.1E-05 (for Prevention) CTUh per tonne of surplus food. The savings decreased the most 
for Incineration, which would be reduced by -8.7%. When considering a fossil marginal electricity, the savings 
were lower for all the scenarios. This might be due to the very toxic by-products produced by nuclear energy 
(Carnegie Mellon University, 2003), which is included in the marginal mix of the baseline scenario. Thus, 
displacing the marginal mix of the baseline scenario leads to higher savings due to lower emissions of toxic 
substances.  
For the environmental category Terrestrial acidification, the results ranged between 1.3 (for Anaerobic 
digestion) and -25.4 (for Prevention) molH+-eq per tonne of surplus food. The savings increased the most for 
Incineration, which raised by 334%. The burdens due to Anaerobic digestion decreased by 26% compared to 
the baseline scenario. Furthermore, when considering electricity produced from natural gas, all the scenarios 
perform better than the baseline, where the electricity is mainly produced by renewable sources. This might be 
due to higher missions of NOx and SOx when burning fossil fuels. 
For the environmental category Eutrophication marine, the results ranged between 2.1 (for Anaerobic 
digestion) to -15.9 (for Prevention) kgN-eq per tonne of surplus food. The savings increased the most for 
Incineration, which raised by ca. 44%. Higher savings are obtained when displacing electricity of fossil origin. 
==PAGE:45==
 
37 
 
In fact, at the power plant, water is used for cooling purposes and it is subsequently discharged in the water 
flows, affecting them negatively (Clearwater, 2017). 
For the environmental category Ecotoxicity, freshwater, the results obtained ranged between -89 (for 
Incineration) to -3259 (for Redistribution) CTUe per tonne of surplus food. The savings increased the most for 
Incineration, which raised by 9.3%. The savings obtained in the varied scenario are higher than the baseline. 
In fact, as mentioned before, water is needed in power plants for cooling purposes. After using it, the water is 
discharged in the water flows affecting their properties and the organisms living in them (Clearwater, 2017). 
For the environmental category Resource depletion, the results obtained ranged between -8642.7 (for 
Incineration) and -18220 (for Prevention) MJ per tonne of surplus food. The savings increased the most for 
Anaerobic digestion followed by Incineration, which raised by 271% and 150%, respectively. When 
considering the marginal electricity of fossil origins, the savings obtained are higher than the ones of the 
baseline scenario as fossil resources are being displaced.  
Overall, the ranking of the scenarios obtained in the baseline did not change: the largest savings, in general, 
come from Prevention.

6.4 Scenario Analysis 
To evaluate the robustness of the hypotheses made in the baseline scenario regarding the composition of the 
surplus food (in terms of specific food items composing the mix), a scenario analysis is performed. This 
analysis consists in varying the shares of the food products (e.g. chicken, pork, cheese, bread, etc.) composing 
the macro-categories, which overall shares are kept constant and equal to the baseline, and in determining the 
extreme worst-best results for each scenario (worst: maximum burden/minimum saving; best: minimum 
burden/maximum saving). The point of this analysis is: i) to illustrate the importance of the composition, in 
terms of specific food products, on the results and ii) to evaluate if the ranking of the scenario may drastically 
change.  
The results are presented in Figure 16. Notice that Depletion of abiotic resources, fossil is addressed as 
Resource depletion is Figure 16.  More details regarding the tested mix of foods and the results, can be found 
in Appendix L and Appendix M, respectively. 
For the environmental category Climate change, the results ranged between -87.4 (for Anaerobic digestion) to 
-2306.8 (for Prevention) kgCO2-eq per tonne of surplus food in the baseline scenario, between -72.7 (for 
Anaerobic digestion) and -1906.9 (for Prevention) kgCO2-eq per tonne of surplus food in the minimum 
scenario, and between -370.6 (for Anaerobic digestion) and -3908.1(for Prevention) kgCO2-eq per tonne of 
surplus food in the maximum scenario.  
For the environmental category Human toxicity, carcinogenic, the results ranged between -1.57E-6 (for 
Incineration) and -5.05E-5 (for Prevention) CTUh per tonne of surplus food in the baseline scenario, between 
-1.33E-6 (for Incineration) and -4.09E-5 (for Prevention) CTUh per tonne of surplus food in the minimum 
scenario, and between -5.14E-6 (for Incineration) and -8.08E-05 (for Redistribution) CTUh per tonne of surplus 
food in the maximum scenario. 
For the environmental category Terrestrial acidification, the results ranged between 1.83 (for Anaerobic 
digestion) to -25.4 (for Prevention) molH+-eq per tonne of surplus food in the baseline scenario, between 2.09 
(for Anaerobic digestion) and -20.1 (for Prevention) molH+-eq per tonne of surplus food in the minimum 
scenario, and between 1.8 (for Anaerobic digestion) and -44.8 (for Redistribution) molH+-eq per tonne of 
surplus food in the maximum scenario. 
For the environmental category Eutrophication marine, the results ranged between 2.2 (for Anaerobic 
digestion) and -15.9 (for Prevention) kgN-eq per tonne of surplus food in the baseline scenario, between 2.3 
(for Anaerobic digestion) and -13.6 (for Prevention) kgN-eq per tonne of surplus food in the minimum 
scenario, and between 1.96 (for Anaerobic digestion) and -36.1 (for Prevention) kgN-eq per tonne of surplus 
food in the minimum scenario. 
For the environmental category Ecotoxicity, freshwater, the results ranged between -81.4 (for Incineration) 
and -3258.8 (for Redistribution) CTUe per tonne of surplus food in the baseline scenario, between -69 (for 
Incineration) and 18119.6 (for Prevention) CTUe per tonne of surplus food in the minimum scenario, and 
between -266 (for Incineration) and -13211.4 (for Prevention) CTUe per tonne of surplus food in the maximum 
scenario. 
For the environmental category Resource depletion, the results ranged between -2450.8 (for Anaerobic 
digestion) and -18220.1 (for Prevention) MJ per tonne of surplus food in the baseline scenario, between -
2153.7 (for Anaerobic digestion) and -14582 (for Prevention) MJ per tonne of surplus food in the minimum 
scenario, and between -7940.9 (for Anaerobic digestion) and -32111.0 (for Prevention) MJ per tonne of surplus 
food in the maximum scenario. 
==PAGE:48==
 
 
40 
 
The ranking of the scenarios is preserved in respect to the baseline, when considering the minimum mix. 
However, when considering the maximum mix, the ranking changes. In fact, the savings obtained for the 
environmental categories Human toxicity, carcinogenic, Terrestrial acidification, Eutrophication marine, and 
Depletion of abiotic resources, fossil in respect to Redistribution, are greater than the ones obtained for 
Prevention. The characterised results of the baseline scenario showed that the savings in respect to the 
environmental category Ecotoxicity, freshwater are larger for the Redistribution scenario. However, when 
considering the maximum mix, the savings obtained for Prevention are larger than the ones obtained for 
Redistribution. In respect to the maximum mix, the ranking changes for four out of six of the environmental 
categories considered. This is due to the tested mix in the two scenarios. Hence, the results are dependent on 
the composition of the mix of surplus food. 
6.4.1 Butter substitution 
The mix considered for the minimum scenario for the environmental category Ecotoxicity, freshwater leads to 
an impact, both for Redistribution (i.e. 10590 CTUe t-1surplus food) and Prevention (i.e. 18120 CTUe t-1 surplus 
food). The food item contributing to the impact is Butter, which represents entirely the macro-category Fresh 
dairy products in the mix considered (i.e. 20% of the total). When producing butter, two by-products are also 
obtained: skimmed milk and buttermilk. The mentioned by-products are sold as protein feeds (Levova, 2014). 
According to Levova (2014) when butter is not produced, the two by-products are substituted on the market 
with a rich protein feed, i.e. soybean. Hence, the avoided production of butter, in both the Redistribution and 
the Prevention scenario, leads to high burdens as a great amount of soybean would have to be produced to 
compensate for the lower presence in the market of skimmed milk and buttermilk (sources of proteins,), 
requiring an extent use of pesticides and herbicides that significantly affects the analysed environmental 
category.

6.5 Normalisation 
Normalisation is usually performed in LCAs to compare results as they are expressed with the same unit (i.e. 
PE). The PE express the share of used resources and release of emissions in the world during one year by one 
person, providing an information on the magnitude of an impact (Stranddorf et al., 2005).  
The normalisation factors applied in the current study are listed in Table 3, section 2.6. The results obtained 
can be found in Table 8. The normalised results show the same ranking as in the baseline scenario. The largest 
savings are obtained, overall for Prevention. Redistribution performs better than Prevention only in respect to 
the environmental category Ecotoxicity, freshwater.

7.Discussion 
Herein, the results obtained in the current study are compared with the ones obtained by Eriksson et al (2015), 
who performed a similar study on food waste generated in Swedish retailers.  
The European Regulations that influence food redistribution are also addressed in this section. The key barriers 
and solutions implemented in the Member States are presented. The barriers that limit the use of surplus food 
as animal feed are discussed. Furthermore, the implementation of food redistribution strategies and regulations 
are discussed both for France and Denmark.  
7.1 Comparison with literature 
An assessment similar to the current study was conducted by Eriksson et al. (2015). However, many are the 
methodological differences that distinguish the two studies, which led to different results. In the study by 
Eriksson et al. (2015), six waste management scenarios were analysed (i.e. landfill, composting, incineration, 
anaerobic digestion, animal feed, and donations) in respect to their impact on Climate change only. The food 
waste that was considered included only six food items (i.e. bananas, wheat bread, iceberg lettuce, grilled 
chicken, and stewing beef). Different are also the marginal technologies/products selected for the scenarios 
considered: (i) the incineration of food waste displaces peat; (ii) the biogas produced from the anaerobic 
digestion substitutes diesel, whereas the digestate substitutes nitrogen and phosphorus fertilizers only; (iii) 
animal feed is produced substituting oat, based on its energy content, with bananas, iceberg lettuce and bread, 
and soybean, based on its protein content, with grilled chicken and stewing beef, even if it is forbidden by the 
law; (iv) bread is identified as the marginal food based on its energy content; v) LUC impacts not included. 
The methodological choice that differentiates the most the two studies is to include or not land use changes in 
the assessment. As mentioned in section 2.5, land use changes account for 12% of the global greenhouse gas 
emissions (Herzog, 2009). On this basis, land use changes should always be included as their contribution 
when dealing with biomass LCAs is crucial to the results. The results obtained in the current study for Animal 
feed and Redistribution show that avoided land use changes contribute by 69% in the former, and by 36% in 
the latter to the total savings, whilst the waste management system contributes only by 13% and 6%, 
respectively.  On the other hand, the results obtained by Eriksson et al. (2015) do not highlight significant 
savings from redistribution of food waste and use as animal feed as land use changes are not included. 
According to Eriksson et al. (2015) anaerobic digestion is the best management option for handling food waste, 
whilst in the current study anaerobic digestion performed as the worst option in respect to Climate change. 
The sensitivity analysis performed by Eriksson et al (2015) on the marginal product/technology showed that 
the choice of what is substituted in the system expansion is crucial. Eriksson et al. (2015) state that the most 
uncertain assumption made in the assessment was the choice of the marginal food and the sensitivity analysis 
showed that it influences greatly on the results. Another important assumption is the one in respect to the 
marginal energy. When energy is produced by fossil sources the impacts are greater than the ones when using 
energy produced by renewables. This is also supported by our results. In respect to Climate change, when the 
marginal electricity was of fossil origin, the savings obtained in the Incineration scenario increased by ca. 
166%, by 448% in the Anaerobic digestion, by ca. 34% in the Animal Feed, and by ca. 15% in the 
Redistribution. The marginal mix of foods was tested in the scenario analysis: the results show that the choice 
of the food items included in the mix highly influences the outcome. However, as a mix of foods is considered, 
it is not possible to evaluate from the obtained results which food items contribute the most to the impacts. 
Eriksson (2015) states that products with large environmental impacts are usually not important in terms of 
mass and vice versa, while monetary values reflect better the environmental impact. This statement could be 
evaluated in the current study with further research. Further, their contribution in terms of mass and if the 
monetary value reflects the environmental impact, could be assessed.  
The waste hierarchy, which was developed within the European Waste Framework Directive (WFD) (Eriksson 
et al., 2015), identifies prevention as the most favoured option when handling waste, followed by redistribution 
==PAGE:52==
 
 
44 
 
(i.e. donation), recycle (i.e. animal feed), recovery (i.e. incineration and anaerobic digestion), and disposal (i.e. 
landfilling). The scenarios considered in the current study only contemplate prevention, redistribution, recycle, 
and recovery as landfilling of food waste should be avoided following EU Regulations (European Commission, 
2016). Overall, the results obtained in the current study support the waste hierarchy. In fact, generally, 
prevention gives the largest savings followed by redistribution of food, recycle as animal feed and recovery. 
According to Eriksson et al. (2012), food losses have unnecessary environmental impacts as the production of 
food products occurs in vain and, in accordance with Oldfield et al. (2016), food production contributes the 
most to the environmental impact. Indeed, the results obtained in this study supports this conclusion, clearly 
showing that food production contributes the most to the impacts. Packaging and transport have little impact 
(e.g below 5% of the total saving of the prevention scenario on GW) on most categories except for Resource 
Depletion; the impact of waste management, though being a non-negligible, is about one order of magnitude 
inferior to the combination of food production and associated LUC impacts. If prevention measures are not 
applicable, redistribution should be implemented not to use limited resources (i.e. water, land, etc.) in vain 
whenever food is still safe and suitable for human consumption. If the surplus food is still safe, but is no longer 
suitable for human consumption, it should be used as animal feed. Finally, if the surplus food is spoilt and is 
no longer suitable neither for human nor for animal consumption, then recovery has to be implemented: heat 
and electricity produced out of food waste can displace the ones of fossil origin. Hence, the results suggest that 
political action should be taken to tackle food waste and implement management strategies that are the most 
environmentally friendly.  
7.2 EU Regulations in respect to food donation 
Food is mainly wasted in households (Halloran et al., 2014); however, the current study is focused on the 
surplus food generated at the retail sector. Supermarkets have an important role in the food supply chain 
(Eriksson, 2015). In fact, the retail sector gathers large amounts of food in a limited number of locations 
(Eriksson, 2015); moreover, retailers are the connection between consumers and producers, and they are 
closely linked to other stages of the food supply chain (Scholz et al., 2014). Thus, the retail sector is a key 
point in reducing food wastage (Scholz et al., 2014). To tackle food waste, food loss, and surplus food it is 
important to identify its causes. The main drivers of the generation of food waste are inadequate technologies, 
education, and consumer preferences, which can be overcome by technological improvements along the entire 
food supply chain, more education, both for consumers and personnel, and shifts in consumption patterns 
(Teuber et al., 2016). The major challenge for the retail sector is to order the correct amount of fresh products, 
which have a short shelf-life and are wasted with very high quantities (Teuber et al., 2016). Furthermore, large 
losses are also created by promotional campaigns, inappropriate packaging, poor storage and handling, 
aesthetic standards and date labelling (Teuber et al., 2016). Moreover, to be able to address the problem of 
food waste, it is necessary to determine the amount of food lost (Lipinksi et al., 2013). To comply with this 
requirement, several methods could be implemented at the wholesale and retail sector, such as scanning/stock-
keeping tools, on-site measurements of mass, or interviewing key personnel (Teuber et al., 2016). Knowing 
the amounts, two different types of measures could be implemented: either prevention or valorisation 
strategies. 
Prevention is the most favoured option within the waste hierarchy and leads to the largest environmental 
savings, as supported by the current study. In accordance with Teuber et al. (2016), the environmental benefits 
induced by prevention are caused by avoided production of food rather than avoided management of waste, as 
supported by this study. The environmental savings due to prevention reported by Teuber et al. (2016), range 
between 0.8 and 4.4 kgCO2-eq per kg of prevented food waste, and are comparable with the result obtained in 
the current study (i.e. 2.3 kgCO2-eq per kg of prevented food waste). To achieve this goal, prevention strategies 
should be applied along the entire food supply chain. According to Teuber et al. (2016) several are the strategies 
that could be implemented to prevent food waste: (i) more communication and cooperation across the food 
supply chain, (ii) optimisation of the cold chain, (iii) modification of aesthetic standards, (iv) improve 
==PAGE:53==
 
45 
 
packaging for highly perishable products to extend their shelf-life, (v) provide more information regarding 
date labels and simplify them.  
If prevention measures cannot be implemented, management strategies have to be applied in accordance with 
the waste hierarchy, as supported by the results of the current study. Redistribution is identified as the best 
option after prevention. In fact, even if it is not intended to reduce food waste and food loss, it can connect 
social security, by increasing the welfare for people in need, and environmental goals, reducing food waste 
(Teuber et al., 2016). Furthermore, minimizing food waste and extending the value of food via redistribution 
meets the goals of the circular economy. Redistribution of food can occur either with direct donations from 
food supply chain donors to charities, or through food banks that store and distribute the donated food to end 
users (e.g. charities) (Hanssen et al., 2014). Food banks, and donation in general, experience the problem of 
receiving unstable amounts of food and lack of variety of the food items donated (Hanssen et al., 2014). 
Moreover, food banks are addressed as food business operators, as they take part in the distribution of food, 
and for this reason they have to comply with the EU Regulations that affect food donation; however, there is 
not a common EU policy for food redistribution (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). 
The laws influencing food donations are: (i) the General Food Law, (ii) the Food Hygiene Package, (iii) Food 
durability and date marking, (iv) the Tax Legislation, (v) the Waste Framework Directive (OвҖҷConnor et al., 
2014). The mentioned laws are discussed below. Their key barriers and solutions proposed by Member States 
are also presented. 
The General Food Law (Regulation (EC) No 178/2002) aims at providing guidelines to assure a coherent 
approach when developing food legislations (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). All the food business operators have to 
comply with the General Food Law, which concerns responsibility, liability, traceability, food health and 
safety and establishes the European Food Safety Authority (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). According to the law, it is 
of main importance to be able to trace food and feed throughout the food chain for hygiene purposes (i.e. 
traceability); moreover, food business operators are responsible for any hygiene problem occurring at the step 
of the food chain under their responsibility (i.e. responsibility); and, if a product is defective (i.e. it does not 
comply with the safety to which people are entitled), producers are liable with damage (i.e. liability) (OвҖҷConnor 
et al., 2014). However, according to the Directive, food donors and food charities are not subject to this 
regulation (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014).  Therefore, food producers and retailers are unwilling to donate food due 
to liability and because, in case of food poisoning, their reputation would be compromised (OвҖҷConnor et al., 
2014). To overcome these problems, several solutions have been implemented in the Member States. In France, 
a transfer slip system was applied to transfer the responsibility on the food charities and prove that the food 
donors comply with the hygiene regulations (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). In Italy, the Good Samaritan legislation 
recognizes food charities as the final consumer; hence, people would not be able to sue food donors (OвҖҷConnor 
et al., 2014).  
The Food Hygiene Package includes three regulations: (i) Regulation (EC) No 852/2004 on the hygiene of 
food products, (ii) Regulation (EC) No 853/2004 on hygiene rules for food products of animal origin, (iii) 
Regulation (EC) No 854/2004 on rules for official controls on products of animal origin for human 
consumption.  The mentioned Regulations have to be observed by all the food business operators, food banks 
included (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). The main barrier of the Food Hygiene Package is the lack of knowledge and 
misunderstanding on the regulations (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). Furthermore, these directives are transported into 
stricter Regulations applied at national level. Hence, to overcome this problem, the European Commission has 
decided to simplify the regulations on food hygiene without jeopardizing food safety (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014).  
The Food durability and date marking (Regulation (EC) No 1169/2011) concerns the conveyance of food 
information to consumers (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). Food business operators have to determine whether, based 
on the food composition, it is more suitable a вҖңuse-byвҖқ or вҖңbest-beforeвҖқ date (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). Food 
products that are no longer safe to eat from a microbiological point of view after a specific date are labelled 
==PAGE:54==
 
 
46 
 
with a вҖңuse-byвҖқ date. On the other hand, food products that are still safe to eat and only loose quality, e.g. 
appearance, are labelled with a вҖңbest-beforeвҖқ date (Teuber et al., 2016). Across the EU, there is a general 
misunderstanding in regards to вҖңbest-beforeвҖқ dates and, therefore, it is thought that food products with this 
label cannot be donated (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). To overcome this problem, Belgium, for example, has 
provided guidelines on how to assess the additional lifetime of food items once they have reached their вҖңbest-
beforeвҖқ date (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014).  
The Tax Legislation (Council Directive 2006/112/EC) states that food donations are taxable if they are made 
by a taxable person and whether the VAT on the purchase of the goods is entirely or partially deductible 
(OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). The legislation states that the taxable amount is calculated as the purchase price (i.e. 
wholesale price) at the moment of the donation corrected by the state of the goods at the time of the donation 
(OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). Furthermore, food donors are not subject to VAT under some circumstances; for 
example, if the food donated is close to its expiration date, then the value of the food item is considered as 
small or zero (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). Hence, if a very low value is attributed to food items close to the вҖңbest-
before/use-byвҖқ date, food donors benefiting from tax credits would be disadvantaged. Thus, to support food 
donation, fiscal incentives have to be applied. France, for example, has set a 60% tax credit to all food donors 
from their income corporate tax (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014).  
The Waste Framework Directive (Directive 2008/98/EC) established that Member States have to apply the 
waste management hierarchy. However, there are no EU guidelines on how to apply it in respect to the 
management of food waste (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). The key barrier of the mentioned Directive is that many 
Member States have implemented fiscal incentives at the lower stages of the hierarchy preventing the 
implementation of food donation. In fact, for many countries it is more expensive to donate food rather than 
sending it to anaerobic digestion (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). To overcome this problem, France, for example, has 
fiscal incentives that support food donation and make it cheaper than anaerobic digestion (OвҖҷConnor et al. 
2014).  
European regulations concerning food donations are available. However, many are the key barriers that prevent 
food donation from being fully implemented. Some countries of the Member States have adopted national 
legislations that facilitate and subsidise food donations. These front-runners should be of inspiration for a 
common and clear regulation addressing food donations in all Member States.  
7.3 Implementation of Animal feed and Anaerobic digestion 
Safe surplus food that is no longer suitable for human consumption can be used to produce animal feed 
(WRAP, 2015). Using surplus food as animal feed could avoid the agricultural inputs (land, water, fertilizers) 
and the correspondent environmental impacts related to crops harvesting for feed production (WRAP, 2015). 
Furthermore, producing animal feed out of surplus food can be beneficial from an economical point of view 
as well; indeed, using surplus food as animal feed can save the costs of sending food waste to either a disposal 
or a treatment facility. 
In respect to the use as animal feed, controlling the safety and quality of the feed is fundamental to protect the 
animalsвҖҷ health (WRAP, 2015). The controls on surplus food, which contains animal by-products (ABPs) and 
is intended for animal feed production, are very strict to prevent the spread of diseases and viruses among 
animals, which could also be a risk for human health (WRAP, 2015). The European Regulations that set the 
rules for animal feed production are harmonised at European level (WRAP, 2015). The Regulations 
implemented are: (i) Regulation 1069/2009 accompanied by Regulation 142/2011, which set the rules on heath 
in respect to ABPs and derived products not intended for human consumption; (ii) Regulation 999/2001, which 
focuses on the prevention, control and extirpation of transmissible spongiform encephalopathies (TSE); (iii) 
Regulation 183/2005, which sets the rules for feed hygiene and impose that all business that either produce, 
use or sell animal feed are registered as a Feed Business Operator (FeBO); (iv) Regulation 767/2009, which 
==PAGE:55==
 
47 
 
sets the requirements for the use of the feed and the placing on the market of the product (WRAP, 2015). In 
respect to the ABPs, the following food products are excluded from feed production: (i) raw meat, fish, eggs 
and shellfish products; (ii) cooked or partially cooked meat, shellfish, fish and alike; (iii) bakery and sweet 
food products containing ruminant gelatine; (iv) any catering waste, whether or not it contains ABPs (WRAP, 
2015). On the other hand, the following food products containing ABPs can be used to feed animals, as they 
are addressed as low risk ABPs: (i) milk, cooked eggs, fats, rennet and non-ruminant gelatine; (ii) food products 
that have undergone pasteurising, baking or cooking (e.g. bakery products, chocolate, pasta, breakfast cereals 
and sweets); (iv) if the products have not been cooked or baked, but they have undergone heat treatments, they 
can still be used for animal feed production (WRAP, 2015). Surplus food could be a high nutritious feed for 
animals, as it contains many valuable nutrients (e.g. carbohydrates). However, it is very important that surplus 
food used for feeding animals is safe and does not contain any of the prohibited ABPs (WRAP, 2015). Because 
of these strict regulations on animal feed production companies often prefer to recover surplus food in a biogas 
plant as it is seen less risky (Eriksen et al., 2017).  
As the regulations both for food donations and the production of animal feed are very stringent, companies 
often prefer to produce biogas out of surplus food. However, anaerobic digestion should be considered as an 
option only when the highest management strategies proposed by the hierarchy have been exhausted (i.e. when 
surplus food is not safe anymore) (Waste Management World, 2014). The main barrier in regards to using food 
waste as a substrate for anaerobic digestion, is food waste source segregation (De Clercq et al., 2017). 
7.4 France 
Amongst the Member States, France is one of the most advanced in respect to food donations accounting for 
12 recognised food banks at national level, e.g. the Red Cross and the French Federation of Food Banks 
(OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). The National Pact against Food Waste was signed by France in 2013 with the aim of 
halving food waste by 2025. Moreover, the Pact aims to look into the legislations related to responsibility and 
liability of food donors. Furthermore, the French Federation of Food Banks has also taken action and outlined 
a best practice guide in respect to the Food Hygiene Package to help food donors and food charities (OвҖҷConnor 
et al., 2014).  
In respect to responsibility and traceability, France has implemented a transfer slip. The slip concerns the state 
(i.e. core temperature, use-by/best-before date) of the food product when donated and is co-signed by the food 
donors and the food banks/charities (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). In respect to liability, food donors and food 
banks/charities subscribe to liability insurance and sign a partnership agreement (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014).  
In respect to the implementation of the waste hierarchy, France has implemented fiscal incentives for food 
donors to reduce their production of bio-waste and prioritise food donation over anaerobic digestion (OвҖҷConnor 
et al., 2014). To incentive food donations, the General Tax Code was implemented and, according to it, 
companies can benefit from a tax credit of 60% of the value of the donation up to 0.5% of the companyвҖҷs 
yearly sales (Mourad, 2015). In addition, if the tax credit was not fully used within the first year due to the 
limit imposed on the turnover, the tax break can be utilised for the following five years (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014).  
Furthermore, if provided by the food donor, the value of the donation also includes the costs of transport and 
storage, which, usually, are critical for food banks/charities due to lack of infrastructures (OвҖҷConnor et al., 
2014).  
PH. is a company that supports the revalorisation of non-food and food products. The company connects 
donors and charities allowing (almost) daily food donations to occur (Buksti et al., 2015). Further, once PH. 
collaborates with the retailers, the food donated is more varied as PH. manages the bureaucratic part of the 
donation by ensuring that potential unsafe food items (e.g. meat) are handled correctly.  PH. has been able to 
promote both prevention and valorisation measures by offering its service and educating the personnel working 
in retailers. Indeed, the data provided by the company showed that once PH. took over, the surplus food 
==PAGE:56==
 
 
48 
 
decreased, this indicating an induced (additional) prevention effect upstream. When PH. started its action in 
September 2015 for the 20 supermarkets that were analysed in the current study, the total food loss amounted 
to 3,342,043 вӮ¬. After one year, the surplus food generated by retailers decreased by 90.2% in September 2016, 
by 91% in October 2016, and by 91.2% in November 2016. Furthermore, the value of the food donated by the 
supermarkets in September 2015 was, in total, 231,336 вӮ¬. After one year of collaboration with PH., the food 
redistributed by retailers increased by 30.8% in September 2016, by 36.8% in October 2016, and by 37% in 
November 2016. Hence, PH. succeeded in applying the waste hierarchy and the objectives of the circular 
economy. 
In respect to biogas production, many are the barriers preventing France to produce biogas. Firstly, food waste 
source separating is a problem that has to be overcome (De Clercq et al., 2017). Secondly, according to De 
Clercq et al. (2017), large amounts of waste with high methane potential leave France and are used for biogas 
production in Germany and Belgium. This is due to the low electricity tariffs in France, which cause scarcity 
of waste for the domestic biogas plants (De Clercq et al., 2017). Hence, France faces still many challenges in 
regards to the implementation of anaerobic digestion. 
7.5 Denmark 
According to Teuber et al. (2016), the annual food loss in Denmark amounts to 716,000 tonne of which 36% 
is generated by retailers. Currently, food redistribution is organised by the food bank FГёdevareBanken, which 
was founded in 2009 (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). Moreover, food redistribution only aims at helping people in 
need and very little attention is put in waste prevention 
The Food Hygiene Package has been transported successfully into national legislations, which are often stricter 
than the ones developed by the EU (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). For example, there are very stringent rules 
regarding the storage temperatures of food items in retailers (Hanssen et al., 2014); this strict approach can 
represent a barrier in food donation. Moreover, food donors are responsible for the food safety until the food 
has reached the final consumer, independently of how the food is stored and distributed, discouraging retailers 
from donating the surplus food (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). 
The donated food is subjected to VAT and food donors can only benefit from tax deduction (OвҖҷConnor et al., 
2014). However, the maximum deductible amount is 14,800 DKK if the food donor is listed and supported by 
the Danish Tax Authorities (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). Hence, fiscal incentives to promote food donation lack in 
Denmark.  
Currently, the main trend in Denmark is to incinerate food and recovery energy from it (Halloran et al., 2014). 
However, according to the Waste Framework Directive, management strategies at higher levels of the waste 
hierarchy should be implemented and promoted with fiscal incentives (OвҖҷConnor et al., 2014). 
Several are the legislative barriers present in Denmark that prevent food from being donated. Further, 
traceability represents another barrier. To overcome it, a clear and structured procedure is needed like, for 
example, the transfer slip implemented in France. Moreover, many are also the logistical problems related to 
food banks. In fact, there is a lack of resources for managing and storing the redistributed food (Hanssen et al., 
2014). Food banks receive discontinuous donations and, usually, large quantities of the same type of food 
(Hanssen et al., 2014). Hence, less stringent national regulations and, mainly, fiscal incentive such as tax 
credits, are required to promote food donations and waste prevention.  
In respect to animal feed production, the key barrier in Denmark is the implementation of the EU Regulation 
on ABPs. In fact, the mentioned regulation is unclear and prevents the use of bio-waste to produce feed for 
animals. For companies, it is less risky to send bio-waste to biogas plants (Eriksen et al., 2017). However, 
according to IEA (2015), there are still insufficient financial incentives for biogas plants, and the regulations 
to which biogas plants are subjected are wide, making its planning very difficult. Moreover, it is difficult for 
==PAGE:57==
 
49 
 
biogas plants to access suitable biomasses (IEA, 2015). Hence, it is necessary to remove the financial barriers, 
simplify the project approval procedure, and find and test new substrates suitable for anaerobic digestion (IEA, 
2015). Further, it is important to note that the Government in Denmark has set the goal that 50% of household 
waste will have to be recycled by 2022 (The Danish Government, 2013). In fact, approximately 80% of 
household waste are incinerated in Denmark, losing the possibility to recycle valuable materials (The Danish 
Government, 2013). Incineration has been for many decades the privileged solution for waste management in 
Denmark (Zero Waste Europe, 2014). However, some studies (e.g. Zero Waste Europe, 2014) support the idea 
that such incineration solution has had negative impacts on the amount of waste produced by Danes, making 
Denmark one of the countries in Europe with the highest generation of waste per capita, somehow not 
incentivizing the reduction of waste and/or the recycling of materials. Indeed, in order to have an efficient 
incineration it is necessary to have a constant supply of waste. Diversion strategies may induce suboptimal use 
of incineration capacity and/or import of waste from other countries, as highlighted in recent scientific 
literature and reports (e.g. Tonini et al. 2016). To achieve the goal of producing less waste and recycle more, 
Denmark has set taxes on incineration (The Danish Government, 2013). This financial strategy can help 
adopting higher management strategies of the waste hierarchy. 
 
 
==PAGE:58==
 
 
50 
 
8.Conclusions 
The main goal of the study was to evaluate the environmental impacts of different management strategies of 
surplus food produced at the retail sector in France. The data regarding the surplus food generated at 
supermarkets was provided by PH. and was expressed as monetary values. Hence, the provided data had to be 
converted to masses to implement a bottom-up approach. Further, to have a detailed description of the surplus 
food in respect to chemical/biochemical/physical/nutritional properties, a database had to be created for this 
purpose.  
The environmental categories addressed in the assessment are Climate change; Human toxicity, carcinogenic; 
Terrestrial acidification; Eutrophication marine, Ecotoxicity, freshwater; and Depletion of abiotic resources, 
fossil. The characterised results obtained at midpoint level for Climate change ranged between -87.4 (for 
Anaerobic digestion) and -2306.8 (for Prevention) kgCO2-eq per tonne of surplus food, for Human toxicity, 
carcinogenic between -1.57E-6 (for Anaerobic digestion) and -5.05E-5 (for Prevention) CTUh per tonne of 
surplus food, for Terrestrial acidification between 1.83 (for Anaerobic digestion) and -25.4 (for Prevention) 
molH+-eq  per tonne of surplus food, for Eutrophication marine between 2.2 (for Anaerobic digestion) and -
15.9 (for Prevention) kgN-eq per tonne of surplus food, for Ecotoxicity freshwater between -81.4 (for 
Incineraation) and -3259 (for Redistribution) CTUe per tonne of surplus food, and for Depletion of abiotic 
resources, abiotic between -2450.8 (for Anaerobic digestion) to -18220 (for Prevention) MJ per tonne of 
surplus food. Overall, the characterised results support the waste hierarchy. Moreover, food production and 
associated land use changes impacts have been identified as the main contributors to the impacts. Preventing 
food waste to occur could avoid the use of limited resource. However, if prevention measures cannot be 
implemented and the food is still safe and suitable for human consumption, redistribution of surplus food has 
to be promoted to avoid the use of resource in vain. Whenever food is safe but it is no longer suitable for 
human consumption, surplus food can be used as animal feed. Finally, if food is no longer suitable neither for 
human nor for animal consumption, recovery has to be implemented: heat and energy produced out of food 
waste can displace the ones of fossil origin. 
The marginal electricity in the baseline scenario represents the French electricity mix, which is mainly 
composed by renewable energies. Thus, a sensitivity analysis was performed on the mentioned parameter. 
Electricity produced from natural gas was used instead as marginal electricity for the Incineration, Anaerobic 
digestion, Animal feed, and Redistribution scenarios. The impact categories that were affected the most by this 
decision were Climate Change and Depletion of abiotic resources, fossil, which are linked to the use of fossil 
energy. The results showed that displacing renewables with energy produced from surplus food does not lead 
to large savings, while displacing fossil fuels with heat and electricity obtained from surplus food might lead 
to higher environmental benefits. Yet, the ranking of the scenarios obtained in the baseline did not change. 
The scenario analysis showed the importance of the food products selected in the mix composition. While 
numerical results may change importantly when changing the shares of food products composing the mixed 
composition, overall the ranking of the scenarios remains unchanged in almost all the environmental categories 
investigated for the minimum mix. However, the ranking did changed for the mix of the maximum scenarios: 
Redistribution performed better in four out of six environmental categories, showing that the composition of 
the mix is not negligible. Yet, for more robust and precise results on environmental savings achieved by 
retailers it is recommendable to have more detailed information on the food products discarded and/or sent to 
redistribution/animal feeding.   
The European Regulations in respect to food donations were discussed. The EU Regulations have been applied 
in the Member States with typically stricter national regulations that make donation of surplus food more 
difficult. Thus, a clear and common regulation is needed in Europe. Further, the European Regulations in 
respect to animal feed production have also been discussed. The regulations both for food donation and animal 
feed production are so strict that companies are afraid to do something illegal and, thus, consider less risky 
==PAGE:59==
 
51 
 
sending surplus food to biogas production, even if it corresponds to a lower stage of the waste hierarchy. 
Furthermore, the implementation of the food donation regulations in France and Denmark were discussed. 
France has improved the traceability of food products through a transfer slip and has set tax credits on food 
donations. Having a fiscal incentive, food donations are preferred to other management strategies. Denmark 
still faces many problems in regards to food donations. The traceability of products is not structured and stricter 
hygiene rules have been set. Furthermore, donors only benefit of tax deductions from food donation. Further, 
there are not fiscal incentives that help the spread of donation. Hence, less strict national regulations and fiscal 
incentives are needed in Denmark to favour food donations over other management strategies. 
 
 
==PAGE:60==
 
 
52 
 
9. Further research 
Further research is needed on the following topics: 
вҖў 
Add the LCIs of missing processes to have a better estimation of the environmental impacts due to food 
production; 
вҖў 
Include the impacts for slaughtering processes for the macro-category Meat, and Poultry to have a better 
estimation of their food production; 
вҖў 
An approach similar to the one used for the displacement of animal feed could be implemented for the 
Redistribution scenario. It would be necessary to identify the most important nutrients for humans; based 
on this a marginal mix could be identified; 
вҖў 
Sensitivity analysis on the specific food products to identify which ones are the ones that contribute the 
most to the environmental impacts. Moreover, it would be possible to verify if the monetary values give a 
better representation of the environmental impacts rather than the masses; 
вҖў 
Research the contribution of packaging production and recycling to the savings/burdens; 
вҖў 
Companies are interested in showing that they are вҖңgreenвҖқ. Hence, they could be provided with a tool that 
would allow them to estimate the environmental impacts of the management strategy that they implement 
based on the study.

Appendix B 
The calculations performed to obtain the substitution factors for the marginal animal feed are herein 
presented. The approach used is based on Tonini et al. (2016). Moreover, the soymeal-palm fruit loop 
is taken into account, accordingly to Dalgaard et al. (2008).  
The characteristics of the mixture of foods coming from the supermarket are presented in Table B1.

Appendix D 
The hypotheses made and the calculations performed on the components of each food item are herein 
presented and explained. 
вқ– Water content 
The water content [%] is equal to вҖңMoistureвҖқ. The quantity is expressed as вҖңg/100g of waterвҖқ and, 
therefore, it is already expressed as a percentage.

вқ– Lower Heating Value (LHV) on dry basis 
The LHVdb [MJ/kgTS] of each food item is calculated as the sum of the LHVdb of all the components 
that contribute to it.  
The properties of the food items that contribute to the total LHVdb are: ash, sucrose, starch, cellulose, 
hemicellulose, pectin, lignin, proteins, lipids, acetic acid, fructose, glucose, lactose, maltose, alcohol, 
other VS. 
The formula used to calculate the total LHVdb of the food item is based on Fowler et al. (2009) and is 
the following: 
100
936
.8
442
.2
H
HHV db
LHV db
пғ—
пғ—
пҖӯ
пҖҪ
 
Where: 
HHVdb= higher heating value [MJ/kgTS] 
 
H= hydrogen content [%] 
To calculate the LHVdb of the mentioned components, the following assumptions were made: 
1. The LHVdb of Other VS, Lactose, Maltose and Added sugars is equal to the one of Sucrose (i.e. 
Saccharose); 
2. The LHVdb of Starch is equal to the one of Cellulose; 
3. The LHVdb of Fructose is equal to the one of Glucose; 
4. The LHVdb of Alcohol is equal to the one of Ethanol. 
To calculate the LHVdb of the single components the formula mentioned above was used.  
To calculate the hydrogen content, the chemical formula is necessary. Based on the number of hydrogen 
present and its molecular weight (MW) [kg/kmol], and the molecular weight of the component it is 
possible to calculate the hydrogen content, expressed as a percentage, as follows:

вқ– Biogenic carbon (рқҗӮрқҗӣрқҗўрқҗЁ) 
The biogenic carbon [%TS] is calculated based on the content of carbon in each component. 
To calculate the carbon content of each component, the same calculations have to be 
performed as for the hydrogen content, which was presented in the section related to the 
LHV. Therefore, it is necessary to know the chemical formula of the component and its 
molecular weight, and the molecular weight of carbon. The following components were 
considered: sucrose, starch, cellulose, hemicellulose, pectin, lignin, proteins, lipids, acetic 
acid, fructose, glucose, lactose, maltose, alcohol, other VS. 
 
To perform the calculations, the following assumptions were made: 
1. The carbon content of Other VS, Lactose, Maltose, Pectin and Added sugars is equal 
to the one of Sucrose (i.e. Saccharose); 
2. The carbon content of Fructose is equal to the one of Glucose; 
3. The carbon content of Starch is equal to the one of Cellulose; 
4. The carbon content of Alcohol is equal to the one of Ethanol. 
 
The formula presented in the LHVdb section to calculate the hydrogen content is valid also 
for the calculation of the share of carbon in each component: 
100

вқ– Correction of added sugars and all sugars 
According to DTU FГёdevareinstituttet (2016), вҖңall sugarsвҖқ accounts for all the known sugars and 
вҖңadded sugarsвҖқ accounts for the refined sucrose, whose amount is not always based on laboratory 
experiments but from readings of the food label. Therefore, it is possible that the amount of вҖңadded 
sugarsвҖқ is not consistent with the amount of вҖңcarbohydratesвҖқ (DTU FГёdevareinstituttet, 2016). Thus, 
there is a double counting of the sucrose. In fact, the total amount obtained summing the amount of 
sucrose, starch, fructose, glucose, lactose, maltose, and delta sugars was not equal to the provided 
amount of carbohydrates for many food items. Thus, it was necessary to correct the amount of sucrose 
provided as it was not stated if it was accounting also for the refined sucrose (i.e. added sugars) or not. 
Hence, an iterative processes was applied: first, it was checked if the amount of added sugars and the 
==PAGE:86==
78 
 
amount of sucrose were equal. If the added sugars and the sucrose were equal the amount of added 
sugars was set to 0, otherwise the amount provided by the Frida Fooddata was kept.

Appendix E 
The data provided by PH. was expressed as wholesale prices. To convert the monetary values into 
masses, conversion factors expressed as [вӮ¬/kg] had to be used and were based on the information 
provided by PH., FranceAgriMer (2016), and Camera di Commercio Roma (2015). It is important to 
notice that only wholesale prices could be used for this calculation.  
Firstly, the wholesale prices expressed as [вӮ¬/kg] were collected from the sources mentioned above for 
at least one food product of each macro-category. The most recent data were considered and, whenever 
it was possible, data from 2015 were applied. For the category Frozen food, literature data were not 
found. Thus, it was necessary to make a hypothesis regarding this macro-category. It was assumed that 
its price was defined as the average price of all the food products that are usually frozen, i.e. fresh pork, 
fruit and vegetables, pasta (as prepared meals), bread, poultry, beef, and pastry. Information were also 
not available for Gourmet. The food products included in this group are high quality products belonging 
to several macro-categories (e.g. Liquids, Meat, Dry sweets, etc.). However, to calculate the conversion 
factor, it was assumed that, based on the information provided by PH., only sweet products having a 
wholesale price greater than 6 вӮ¬/kg were considered to calculate an average price. The food products 
considered for each macro-category and the sources used are listed in Table E1. Notice that the sources 
are reported as follows: [1] (i.e. PH.), [2] (i.e. FranceAgriMer), [3] (Camera di Commercio Roma).

To have a detailed characterization of the surplus food, it was necessary to investigate the French food 
consumption pattern. Information were found for all the macro-categories except for Frozen food. 
Therefore, it was assumed that all the food products considered for its average wholesale price were 
consumed equally. The data collected on the food habits in France was based on the study by the Agence 
FranГ§aise de SГ©curitГ© Sanitaire des Aliments (2009), Interfel (2017), Maison du Lait (2017), Les 
Fabricants de Biscuits & GГўteaux de France (2015) and FranceAgriMer (2014).  The INCAA 2 study 
reported the consumption patterns of adults (age between 18 and 79), and children (age between 3 and 
17). Therefore, the data were weighted according to the population of France in 2007 based on the data 
provided by the Institut national de la statistique et des Г©tudes Г©conomiques (2017). The consumption 
patterns of fruit and vegetables were based on the study by Interfel. The data were expressed either as 
kg/person/year or as kg/household/year, where a household is composed by 2.3 people (INSEE, 2017). 
As the information reported by Interfel was collected between 2010 and 2015, it was decided to weight 
it on the population of 2013 based on data provided by The World Bank (2017). The results obtained 
are summarized in Table E2.

Appendix F 
PH. provided both the monetary values of the donated food per macro-category and the share of donated 
food per macro-category. Combining this two information, it was possible to calculate the surplus food 
per macro-category of each supermarket. 
The monetary values of the donated food in September, October and November 2016 from the 20 
supermarkets are presented in Table F1, F2 and F3. Notice that the names of the supermarkets are not 
available due to confidentiality reasons and that the values are negative as they represent unsold goods 
and, thus, a loss for the supermarkets.

For the LCA, the surplus food of an average supermarket was considered. The average supermarket is 
herein defined as the average of the surplus food generated by the 20 supermarkets over the three 
months. The average of the 20 supermarkets for September, October, and November 2016 together with 
the average over the three months, are reported in Table F10.

Appendix G 
The total surplus food includes both the food and its packaging, accordingly to the functional unit of the study. 
The amount of packaging had to be calculated and the assumptions were based on Tonini et al. (2017). It is 
important to notice that in France, fruit and vegetables are sold without any packaging. Thus, the amount of 
packaging for Fruit and Vegetables was set to 0 kg.  
The type of packaging of each food item, the weight of the packaging [kgpackaging/kgtotal food loss], the total surplus 
food [kg] per macro-category and per each food product, the amount of packaging [kg] per macro-category 
and per food product, and the surplus food of each food product [kg] are presented in Table G1.

Appendix J 
The list of the processes for the production and transport of the food products are presented in Table J1. All 
the processes are based on Ecoinvent 3.3, Consequential. It is important to note that the transport of the 
processes was built based on the correspondent вҖңmarket forвҖқ process. It was necessary to recalculate the 
transport of Fruit and Vegetables as the losses from the retail sector to households are also considered and are 
equal to 12%. The transport was scaled to only the share of food that is actually transported and, therefore, the 
losses were excluded to avoid double-countings.

Appendix K 
To calculate the uncertainty of the parameters, the method presented by Bisinella et al. (2016) was applied. It 
was assumed that the parameters had a triangular distribution, whose domain is characterised by a minimum, 
a maximum, and a mode. Being an arbitrary determined triangular distribution, the mode is equal to the 
average. The variance of the distribution is calculated as follows: 
пҖЁ

1
пҒі
 
Where: 
a = lower boundary (a<b) 
 
b = upper boundary 
 
c = mode (a вүӨ cвүӨ b) 
If the mode is equal to one of the two boundaries, the triangular is skewed either to the left (c=a) or to the right 
(c=b) and the variance has to be calculated as follows:

пҖ©

пҒі
 
The domain of the considered parameters was set as follows: 
вҖў 
Calculated parameters (i.e. land use changes, soybean, maize grain, palm fruit, redistribution 
substitution coefficients, transport, refrigeration, biogas yield, share of food going to anaerobic 
digestion) were varied by Вұ20%. 
вҖў 
Parameters from the literature (i.e. efficiency of electricity and heat recovery for incineration, 
efficiency of electricity and heat recovery for anaerobic digestion, share of intensification for land use 
changes, management option) were constrained to the given boundary.  
 
The list of the parameters with the value used in the baseline scenario, together with the lower and upper 
boundary considered and the calculated variance, are presented in Table K1. It is important to notice that the 
amount of food and packaging was not varied to fulfil the functional unit (i.e. 1 tonne of surplus food and the 
correspondent packaging).

Appendix L 
The composition of the mix of food items considered in the scenario analysis are herein presented. 
For the Incineration scenario, the mix was composed considering the LHV of the food products in the mix of 
the baseline scenario. Based on this property, the mix was built selecting for each macro-category the food 
products with the highest/lowest LHV. The amount of surplus food (expressed in kg) of each macro-category 
had to be preserved. Based on the food products selected for each macro-category, the amount of packaging 
required was calculated, according to Table G1 in Appendix G.  The results obtained for the minimum scenario 
are presented in Table L1. The share of the packaging on the total loss is 2.2%.

For the Anaerobic digestion scenario, the mix was composed considering the CH4 potential of the food items 
in the mix of the baseline scenario. Based on this property, the mix was built selecting for each macro-category 
the food items with the highest/lowest methane potential. The amount of surplus food (expressed in kg) of 
each macro-category had to be preserved. Based on the food items selected for each macro-category, the 
amount of packaging required was calculated, according to Table G1 in Appendix G.  The results obtained for 
the minimum scenario are presented in Table L3. The share of the packaging on the total loss is 2.2%. A 
different mix was used for Eutrophication marine, and another one for Human toxicity, carcinogenic and 
Ecotoxicity, freshwater. For Eutrophication marine, the same mix as in the Prevention scenario was used, 
while for Human toxicity and Ecotoxicity, freshwater a different mix was considered based on the phosphorus 
content of the food products and it is presented in Table L4.

For the Animal feed scenario, the mix was composed considering the food products that lead to the 
minimum/maximum displacement of the marginal feeds. Only Fruit and Vegetables and Bakery are suitable 
for animal consumption. Bakery includes only one food products, i.e. bread, whilst Fruit and Vegetables 
include several food products. Thus, based on the criterion of the minimum/maximum displacement of the 
marginal feeds, food products belonging to the macro-category Fruit and Vegetables were selected. The 
amount of surplus food (expressed in kg) of the mentioned macro-category had to be preserved. The 
characterisation of the other macro-categories (e.g. the food items included in Dry sweets/Dry savoury, etc.) 
were not changed. The food product belonging to the macro-category Fruit and Vegetables contributing to the 
minimum substitution of the marginal feeds was pear: only 0.024 kg of soybean, 0.2 kg of maize grain, and 
0.017 kg of palm fruit are displaced. On the other hand, walnut contributed to the highest substitution of the 
marginal feeds: 0.44 kg of soybean, 1.8 kg of maize grain, and 0.32 kg of palm fruit are displaced when feeding 
animals with the mentioned food item. 
For the Redistribution scenario, the mix was composed considering the food items that had the lowest/highest 
impact on each environmental category. To define the marginal mix, the classification base on the WHO (2012) 
was used and four categories were considered: Fruit and Vegetables, Grain, Meat, and Dairy. According to 
this classification the food items were selected. The mix used for each impact category is presented in Table 
L7. The packaging was calculated according to Appendix G.

minimum/maximum impact on each environmental category. The amount of surplus food (expressed in kg) of 
each macro-category had to be preserved. Based on the food products selected for each macro-category, the 
amount of packaging required was calculated, according to the method presented in Appendix G. The food 
products considered, with the minimum effect on the environmental category Climate change are presented in 
Table L8. The amount of packaging on the total food loss is 2.2%.
